<h1 class="firstHeading mw-first-heading" id="firstHeading"><span class="mw-page-title-main">Black Death</span></h1>
<div class="vector-body" id="bodyContent">
<div class="noprint" id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
<div id="contentSub"></div>
<div id="contentSub2"></div>
<div id="jump-to-nav"></div>
<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#mw-head">Jump to navigation</a>
<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#searchInput">Jump to search</a>
<div class="mw-body-content mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">1346–1353 pandemic in Eurasia and North Africa</div>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1033289096">.mw-parser-output .hatnote{font-style:italic}.mw-parser-output div.hatnote{padding-left:1.6em;margin-bottom:0.5em}.mw-parser-output .hatnote i{font-style:normal}.mw-parser-output .hatnote+link+.hatnote{margin-top:-0.5em}</style><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">For other uses, see <a class="mw-disambig" href="/wiki/Black_Death_(disambiguation)" title="Black Death (disambiguation)">Black Death (disambiguation)</a>.</div>
<p class="mw-empty-elt">
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1066479718">.mw-parser-output .infobox-subbox{padding:0;border:none;margin:-3px;width:auto;min-width:100%;font-size:100%;clear:none;float:none;background-color:transparent}.mw-parser-output .infobox-3cols-child{margin:auto}.mw-parser-output .infobox .navbar{font-size:100%}body.skin-minerva .mw-parser-output .infobox-header,body.skin-minerva .mw-parser-output .infobox-subheader,body.skin-minerva .mw-parser-output .infobox-above,body.skin-minerva .mw-parser-output .infobox-title,body.skin-minerva .mw-parser-output .infobox-image,body.skin-minerva .mw-parser-output .infobox-full-data,body.skin-minerva .mw-parser-output .infobox-below{text-align:center}</style><table class="infobox"><tbody><tr><th class="infobox-above" colspan="2" style="background:#FFCCCB">Black Death</th></tr><tr><td class="infobox-image" colspan="2" style="border-bottom:#aaa 1px solid;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:1346-1353_spread_of_the_Black_Death_in_Europe_map.svg"><img alt="The spread of the Black Death in Europe and the Near East (1346–1353)" data-file-height="866" data-file-width="992" decoding="async" height="349" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4e/1346-1353_spread_of_the_Black_Death_in_Europe_map.svg/400px-1346-1353_spread_of_the_Black_Death_in_Europe_map.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4e/1346-1353_spread_of_the_Black_Death_in_Europe_map.svg/600px-1346-1353_spread_of_the_Black_Death_in_Europe_map.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4e/1346-1353_spread_of_the_Black_Death_in_Europe_map.svg/800px-1346-1353_spread_of_the_Black_Death_in_Europe_map.svg.png 2x" width="400"/></a><div class="infobox-caption" style="text-align:left;"><div class="center" style="width:auto; margin-left:auto; margin-right:auto;">The spread of the Black Death in <a href="/wiki/Europe" title="Europe">Europe</a>, <a href="/wiki/North_Africa" title="North Africa">North Africa</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Near_East" title="Near East">Near East</a> (1346–1353) </div></div></td></tr><tr><th class="infobox-label" scope="row">Disease</th><td class="infobox-data"><a href="/wiki/Bubonic_plague" title="Bubonic plague">Bubonic plague</a></td></tr><tr><th class="infobox-label" scope="row">Location</th><td class="infobox-data"><a href="/wiki/Eurasia" title="Eurasia">Eurasia</a> and <a href="/wiki/North_Africa" title="North Africa">North Africa</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-plague-drug-resistant_1-0"><a href="#cite_note-plague-drug-resistant-1">[1]</a></sup></td></tr><tr><th class="infobox-label" scope="row">Date</th><td class="infobox-data">1346–1353</td></tr><tr><th class="infobox-label" scope="row"><div style="display: inline-block; line-height: 1.2em; padding: .1em 0;">Deaths</div></th><td class="infobox-data">75,000,000–200,000,000 (estimated)</td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>The <b>Black Death</b> (also known as the <b>Pestilence</b>, the <b>Great Mortality</b> or simply the <b>Plague</b>)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2"><a href="#cite_note-2">[a]</a></sup> was a <a href="/wiki/Bubonic_plague" title="Bubonic plague">bubonic plague</a> <a href="/wiki/Pandemic" title="Pandemic">pandemic</a> occurring in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Western_Eurasia" title="Western Eurasia">Western Eurasia</a> and <a href="/wiki/North_Africa" title="North Africa">North Africa</a> from 1346 to 1353. It is the <a href="/wiki/List_of_epidemics" title="List of epidemics">most fatal pandemic</a> recorded in human history, causing the deaths of <span class="nowrap">75–200 million</span> people,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-plague-drug-resistant_1-1"><a href="#cite_note-plague-drug-resistant-1">[1]</a></sup> peaking in <a href="/wiki/Europe" title="Europe">Europe</a> from 1347 to 1351.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lead_numbers_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-lead_numbers-3">[2]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[3]</a></sup> Bubonic plague is caused by the <a href="/wiki/Bacteria" title="Bacteria">bacterium</a> <i><a href="/wiki/Yersinia_pestis" title="Yersinia pestis">Yersinia pestis</a></i> spread by fleas, but it can also take a secondary form where it is spread by person-to-person contact via <a href="/wiki/Aerosol" title="Aerosol">aerosols</a> causing <a href="/wiki/Septicemic_plague" title="Septicemic plague">septicaemic</a> or <a href="/wiki/Pneumonic_plague" title="Pneumonic plague">pneumonic plagues</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHaenschBianucciSignoliRajerison2010_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHaenschBianucciSignoliRajerison2010-5">[4]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[5]</a></sup>
</p><p>The Black Death was the beginning of the <a href="/wiki/Second_plague_pandemic" title="Second plague pandemic">second plague pandemic</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[6]</a></sup> The plague created religious, social and economic upheavals, with profound effects on the course of European history.
</p><p>The origin of the Black Death is disputed. The pandemic originated either in <a href="/wiki/Central_Asia" title="Central Asia">Central Asia</a> or <a href="/wiki/East_Asia" title="East Asia">East Asia</a> but its first definitive appearance was in <a href="/wiki/Crimea" title="Crimea">Crimea</a> in 1347.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-plague-drug-resistant_1-2"><a href="#cite_note-plague-drug-resistant-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lead_origin_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-lead_origin-8">[7]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Accuracy_dispute#Disputed_statement" title="Wikipedia:Accuracy dispute"><span title="The material near this tag is possibly inaccurate or nonfactual. (July 2022)">dubious</span></a> <span class="metadata"> – <a href="/wiki/Talk:Black_Death#Dubious" title="Talk:Black Death">discuss</a></span></i>]</sup> From Crimea, it was most likely carried by <a href="/wiki/Oriental_rat_flea" title="Oriental rat flea">fleas</a> living on the <a href="/wiki/Black_rat" title="Black rat">black rats</a> that travelled on <a href="/wiki/Republic_of_Genoa" title="Republic of Genoa">Genoese</a> ships, spreading through the <a href="/wiki/Mediterranean_Basin" title="Mediterranean Basin">Mediterranean Basin</a> and reaching <a href="/wiki/North_Africa" title="North Africa">North Africa</a>, <a href="/wiki/Western_Asia" title="Western Asia">Western Asia</a>, and the rest of Europe via <a href="/wiki/Constantinople" title="Constantinople">Constantinople</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sicily" title="Sicily">Sicily</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Italian_Peninsula" title="Italian Peninsula">Italian Peninsula</a>. There is evidence that once it came ashore, the Black Death mainly spread person-to-person as pneumonic plague, thus explaining the quick inland spread of the epidemic, which was faster than would be expected if the primary <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vector_(epidemiology)" title="Vector (epidemiology)">vector</a> was <a href="/wiki/Rat_flea" title="Rat flea">rat fleas</a> causing bubonic plague.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESnowden201949–53_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESnowden201949–53-9">[8]</a></sup> In 2022, it was discovered that there was a sudden surge of deaths in what is today <a href="/wiki/Kyrgyzstan" title="Kyrgyzstan">Kyrgyzstan</a> from the Black Death in the late 1330s; when combined with genetic evidence, this implies that the initial spread may not have been due to <a href="/wiki/Mongol_invasions_and_conquests" title="Mongol invasions and conquests">Mongol conquests</a> in the 14th century, as previously speculated.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[9]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Spyrou_1–7_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-Spyrou_1–7-11">[10]</a></sup>
</p><p>The Black Death was the second great natural disaster to strike Europe during the <a href="/wiki/Crisis_of_the_Late_Middle_Ages" title="Crisis of the Late Middle Ages">Late Middle Ages</a> (the first one being the <a href="/wiki/Great_Famine_of_1315%E2%80%931317" title="Great Famine of 1315–1317">Great Famine of 1315–1317</a>) and is estimated to have killed 30 percent to 60 percent of the European population, as well as about one-third of the population of the Middle East.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAberth20109–13_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAberth20109–13-12">[11]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAlchon200321_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAlchon200321-13">[12]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[13]</a></sup> The plague might have reduced the <a href="/wiki/World_population" title="World population">world population</a> from <abbr title="circa">c.</abbr><span style="white-space:nowrap;"> 475 million</span> to 350–375 million in the 14th century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[14]</a></sup> There were further outbreaks throughout the Late Middle Ages and, with other contributing factors (the <a href="/wiki/Crisis_of_the_Late_Middle_Ages" title="Crisis of the Late Middle Ages">Crisis of the Late Middle Ages</a>), the European population did not regain its level in 1300 until 1500.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[b]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[15]</a></sup> Outbreaks of the plague recurred around the world until the early 19th century.
</p>

</div>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Names"><span id="Etymology"></span><span id="Name"></span><span id="Naming"></span>Names</span></h2>
<p>European writers contemporary with the plague described the disease in Latin as <span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">pestis</i></span> or <span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">pestilentia</i></span>, 'pestilence'; <span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">epidemia</i></span>, 'epidemic'; <span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">mortalitas</i></span>, 'mortality'.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-:1-18">[16]</a></sup> In English prior to the 18th century, the event was called the "pestilence" or "great pestilence", "the plague" or the "great death".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_18-1"><a href="#cite_note-:1-18">[16]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBennettHollister2006326_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBennettHollister2006326-19">[17]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[18]</a></sup> Subsequent to the pandemic "the <i>furste moreyn</i>" (first <a href="/wiki/Murrain" title="Murrain">murrain</a>) or "first pestilence" was applied, to distinguish the mid-14th century phenomenon from other infectious diseases and epidemics of plague.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_18-2"><a href="#cite_note-:1-18">[16]</a></sup>
</p><p>The 1347 pandemic plague was not referred to specifically as "black" in the time of occurrence in any European language, though the expression "black death" had occasionally been applied to fatal disease beforehand.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_18-3"><a href="#cite_note-:1-18">[16]</a></sup> "Black death" was not used to describe the plague pandemic in English until the 1750s; the term is first attested in 1755, where it translated <a href="/wiki/Danish_language" title="Danish language">Danish</a>: <i lang="da">den sorte død</i>, <small><a href="/wiki/Literal_translation" title="Literal translation">lit.</a> </small>'the black death'.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_18-4"><a href="#cite_note-:1-18">[16]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[19]</a></sup> This expression as a proper name for the pandemic had been popularized by Swedish and Danish chroniclers in the 15th and early 16th centuries, and in the 16th and 17th centuries was transferred to other languages as a <a href="/wiki/Calque" title="Calque">calque</a>: <a href="/wiki/Icelandic_language" title="Icelandic language">Icelandic</a>: <i lang="is">svarti dauði</i>, <a href="/wiki/German_language" title="German language">German</a>: <i lang="de">der schwarze Tod</i>, and <a href="/wiki/French_language" title="French language">French</a>: <i lang="fr">la mort noire</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:2_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-:2-22">[20]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[21]</a></sup> Previously, most European languages had named the pandemic a variant or calque of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Latin_language" title="Latin language">Latin</a>: <i lang="la">magna mortalitas</i>, <small><a href="/wiki/Literal_translation" title="Literal translation">lit.</a> </small>'Great Death'.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_18-5"><a href="#cite_note-:1-18">[16]</a></sup>
</p><p>The phrase 'black death' – describing <a href="/wiki/Thanatos" title="Thanatos">Death</a> as black – is very old. <a href="/wiki/Homer" title="Homer">Homer</a> used it in the <a href="/wiki/Odyssey" title="Odyssey">Odyssey</a> to describe the monstrous <a href="/wiki/Scylla" title="Scylla">Scylla</a>, with her mouths "full of black Death" (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ancient_Greek_language" title="Ancient Greek language">Ancient Greek</a>: <span lang="grc">πλεῖοι μέλανος Θανάτοιο</span>, <small><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Romanization_of_Ancient_Greek" title="Romanization of Ancient Greek">romanized</a>: </small><span title="Ancient Greek-language romanization"><i lang="grc-Latn">pleîoi mélanos Thanátoio</i></span>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[22]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:2_22-1"><a href="#cite_note-:2-22">[20]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Seneca_the_Younger" title="Seneca the Younger">Seneca the Younger</a> may have been the first to describe an epidemic as 'black death', (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Latin_language" title="Latin language">Latin</a>: <i lang="la">mors atra</i>) but only in reference to the acute lethality and dark <a href="/wiki/Prognosis" title="Prognosis">prognosis</a> of disease.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[23]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:2_22-2"><a href="#cite_note-:2-22">[20]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_18-6"><a href="#cite_note-:1-18">[16]</a></sup> The 12th–13th century French physician <a href="/wiki/Gilles_de_Corbeil" title="Gilles de Corbeil">Gilles de Corbeil</a> had already used <i><span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">atra mors</i></span></i> to refer to a "pestilential fever" (<span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">febris pestilentialis</i></span>) in his work <i>On the Signs and Symptoms of Diseases</i> (<span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">De signis et symptomatibus aegritudium</i></span>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:2_22-3"><a href="#cite_note-:2-22">[20]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[24]</a></sup> The phrase <span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">mors nigra</i></span>, 'black death', was used in 1350 by Simon de Covino (or Couvin), a Belgian astronomer, in his poem "On the Judgement of the Sun at a Feast of Saturn" (<span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">De judicio Solis in convivio Saturni</i></span>), which attributes the plague to an astrological <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Conjunction_(astrology)" title="Conjunction (astrology)">conjunction</a> of Jupiter and Saturn.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[25]</a></sup> His use of the phrase is not connected unambiguously with the plague pandemic of 1347 and appears to refer to the fatal outcome of disease.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_18-7"><a href="#cite_note-:1-18">[16]</a></sup>
</p><p>The historian Cardinal <a href="/wiki/Francis_Aidan_Gasquet" title="Francis Aidan Gasquet">Francis Aidan Gasquet</a> wrote about the Great Pestilence in 1893<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGasquet1893_28-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGasquet1893-28">[26]</a></sup> and suggested that it had been "some form of the ordinary Eastern or bubonic plague".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChristakosOleaSerreWang2005110–14_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChristakosOleaSerreWang2005110–14-29">[27]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[c]</a></sup> In 1908, Gasquet claimed that use of the name <i><span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">atra mors</i></span></i> for the 14th-century epidemic first appeared in a 1631 book on Danish history by <a href="/wiki/Johannes_Isacius_Pontanus" title="Johannes Isacius Pontanus">J. I. Pontanus</a>: "Commonly and from its effects, they called it the black death" (<i><span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">Vulgo &amp; ab effectu atram mortem vocitabant</i></span></i>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGasquet19087_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGasquet19087-31">[28]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[29]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Previous_plague_epidemics">Previous plague epidemics</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Yersinia_pestis_fluorescent.jpeg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2268" data-file-width="2294" decoding="async" height="218" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/15/Yersinia_pestis_fluorescent.jpeg/220px-Yersinia_pestis_fluorescent.jpeg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/15/Yersinia_pestis_fluorescent.jpeg/330px-Yersinia_pestis_fluorescent.jpeg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/15/Yersinia_pestis_fluorescent.jpeg/440px-Yersinia_pestis_fluorescent.jpeg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Yersinia_pestis_fluorescent.jpeg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i><a href="/wiki/Yersinia_pestis" title="Yersinia pestis">Yersinia pestis</a></i> (200 × magnification), the bacterium which causes plague<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[30]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Plague_(disease)" title="Plague (disease)">Plague (disease)</a> and <a href="/wiki/First_plague_pandemic" title="First plague pandemic">First plague pandemic</a></div>
<p>Research from 2017 suggests plague first infected humans in Europe and Asia in the <a href="/wiki/Late_Neolithic" title="Late Neolithic">Late Neolithic</a>-<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Early_Bronze_Age" title="Early Bronze Age">Early Bronze Age</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAndrades_ValtueñaMittnikKeyHaak2017_34-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAndrades_ValtueñaMittnikKeyHaak2017-34">[31]</a></sup> Research in 2018 found evidence of <i><a href="/wiki/Yersinia_pestis" title="Yersinia pestis">Yersinia pestis</a></i> in an ancient Swedish tomb, which may have been associated with the "<a href="/wiki/Neolithic_decline" title="Neolithic decline">Neolithic decline</a>" around 3000 BCE, in which European populations fell significantly.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[32]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERascovanSjögrenKristiansenNielsen2019_36-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERascovanSjögrenKristiansenNielsen2019-36">[33]</a></sup> This <i>Y. pestis</i> may have been different from more modern types, with bubonic plague transmissible by fleas first known from Bronze Age remains near <a href="/wiki/Samara" title="Samara">Samara</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESpyrouTukhbatovaWangValtueña2018_37-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESpyrouTukhbatovaWangValtueña2018-37">[34]</a></sup>
</p><p>The symptoms of bubonic plague are first attested in a <a href="/wiki/Literary_fragment" title="Literary fragment">fragment</a> of <a href="/wiki/Rufus_of_Ephesus" title="Rufus of Ephesus">Rufus of Ephesus</a> preserved by <a href="/wiki/Oribasius" title="Oribasius">Oribasius</a>; these ancient medical authorities suggest bubonic plague had appeared in the <a href="/wiki/Roman_Empire" title="Roman Empire">Roman Empire</a> before the reign of <a href="/wiki/Trajan" title="Trajan">Trajan</a>, six centuries before arriving at <a href="/wiki/Pelusium" title="Pelusium">Pelusium</a> in the reign of <a href="/wiki/Justinian_I" title="Justinian I">Justinian I</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGreen201531ff_38-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGreen201531ff-38">[35]</a></sup> In 2013, researchers confirmed earlier speculation that the cause of the <a href="/wiki/Plague_of_Justinian" title="Plague of Justinian">Plague of Justinian</a> (541–542 CE, with recurrences until 750) was <i>Y</i>. <i>pestis</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[36]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[37]</a></sup> This is known as the <a href="/wiki/First_plague_pandemic" title="First plague pandemic">first plague pandemic</a>. In 610, the Chinese physician <a href="/wiki/Chao_Yuanfang" title="Chao Yuanfang">Chao Yuanfang</a> described a "malignant bubo" "coming in abruptly with high fever together with the appearance of a bundle of nodes beneath the tissue."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[38]</a></sup> The Chinese physician Sun Simo who died in 652 also mentioned a "malignant bubo" and plague that was common in <a href="/wiki/Lingnan" title="Lingnan">Lingnan</a> (<a href="/wiki/Guangzhou" title="Guangzhou">Guangzhou</a>). Ole Jørgen Benedictow believes that this indicates it was an offshoot of the first plague pandemic which made its way eastward to Chinese territory by around 600.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[39]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="14th-century_plague">14th-century plague</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Causes">Causes</span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_theory">Early theory</span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Theories_of_the_Black_Death" title="Theories of the Black Death">Theories of the Black Death</a></div>
<p>The most authoritative contemporary account is found in a report from the medical faculty in Paris to <a href="/wiki/Philip_VI_of_France" title="Philip VI of France">Philip VI of France</a>. It blamed the heavens, in the form of a <a href="/wiki/Conjunction_(astronomy)" title="Conjunction (astronomy)">conjunction</a> of three planets in 1345 that caused a "great pestilence in the air" (<a href="/wiki/Miasma_theory" title="Miasma theory">miasma theory</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHorrox1994159_43-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHorrox1994159-43">[40]</a></sup> Muslim religious scholars taught that the pandemic was a "martyrdom and mercy" from God, assuring the believer's place in paradise. For non-believers, it was a punishment.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKelly2005_44-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKelly2005-44">[41]</a></sup> Some Muslim doctors cautioned against trying to prevent or treat a disease sent by God. Others adopted preventive measures and treatments for plague used by Europeans. These Muslim doctors also depended on the writings of the ancient Greeks.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[42]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[43]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Predominant_modern_theory">Predominant modern theory</span></h4>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1096954695/mw-parser-output/.tmulti">.mw-parser-output .tmulti .multiimageinner{display:flex;flex-direction:column}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .trow{display:flex;flex-direction:row;clear:left;flex-wrap:wrap;width:100%;box-sizing:border-box}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .tsingle{margin:1px;float:left}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .theader{clear:both;font-weight:bold;text-align:center;align-self:center;background-color:transparent;width:100%}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .thumbcaption{background-color:transparent}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .text-align-left{text-align:left}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .text-align-right{text-align:right}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .text-align-center{text-align:center}@media all and (max-width:720px){.mw-parser-output .tmulti .thumbinner{width:100%!important;box-sizing:border-box;max-width:none!important;align-items:center}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .trow{justify-content:center}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .tsingle{float:none!important;max-width:100%!important;box-sizing:border-box;text-align:center}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .tsingle .thumbcaption{text-align:left}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .trow>.thumbcaption{text-align:center}}</style><div class="thumb tmulti tright"><div class="thumbinner multiimageinner" style="width:402px;max-width:402px"><div class="trow"><div class="tsingle" style="width:216px;max-width:216px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Xenopsylla_chepsis_(oriental_rat_flea).jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="381" data-file-width="574" decoding="async" height="142" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/25/Xenopsylla_chepsis_%28oriental_rat_flea%29.jpg/214px-Xenopsylla_chepsis_%28oriental_rat_flea%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/25/Xenopsylla_chepsis_%28oriental_rat_flea%29.jpg/321px-Xenopsylla_chepsis_%28oriental_rat_flea%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/25/Xenopsylla_chepsis_%28oriental_rat_flea%29.jpg/428px-Xenopsylla_chepsis_%28oriental_rat_flea%29.jpg 2x" width="214"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption">The <a href="/wiki/Oriental_rat_flea" title="Oriental rat flea">Oriental rat flea</a> (<i>Xenopsylla cheopis</i>) engorged with blood. This <a href="/wiki/Species" title="Species">species</a> of flea is the primary <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vector_(epidemiology)" title="Vector (epidemiology)">vector</a> for the transmission of <i><a href="/wiki/Yersinia_pestis" title="Yersinia pestis">Yersinia pestis</a></i>, the organism responsible for spreading bubonic plague in most plague epidemics. Both male and female fleas <a href="/wiki/Hematophagy" title="Hematophagy">feed on blood</a> and can transmit the infection.</div></div><div class="tsingle" style="width:182px;max-width:182px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Flea_infected_with_yersinia_pestis.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="189" data-file-width="250" decoding="async" height="136" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c1/Flea_infected_with_yersinia_pestis.jpg/180px-Flea_infected_with_yersinia_pestis.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/c/c1/Flea_infected_with_yersinia_pestis.jpg 1.5x" width="180"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption">Oriental rat flea (<i>Xenopsylla cheopis</i>) infected with the <i><a href="/wiki/Yersinia_pestis" title="Yersinia pestis">Yersinia pestis</a></i> <a href="/wiki/Bacteria" title="Bacteria">bacterium</a> which appears as a dark mass in the gut. The foregut (<i>proventriculus</i>) of this flea is blocked by a <i>Y. pestis</i> <a href="/wiki/Biofilm" title="Biofilm">biofilm</a>; when the flea feeds on an uninfected <a href="/wiki/Host_(biology)" title="Host (biology)">host</a> <i>Y. pestis</i> is regurgitated into the wound, causing infection.</div></div></div></div></div>
<p>Due to <a href="/wiki/Crisis_of_the_Late_Middle_Ages#Climate_change_and_plague_pandemic_correlation" title="Crisis of the Late Middle Ages">climate change in Asia</a>, rodents began to flee the dried-out grasslands to more populated areas, spreading the disease.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETignorBrownLiuShaw2014407_47-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETignorBrownLiuShaw2014407-47">[44]</a></sup> The plague disease, caused by the bacterium <i><a href="/wiki/Yersinia_pestis" title="Yersinia pestis">Yersinia pestis</a></i>, is <a href="/wiki/Enzootic" title="Enzootic">enzootic</a> (commonly present) in populations of fleas carried by ground <a href="/wiki/Rodent" title="Rodent">rodents</a>, including <a href="/wiki/Marmot" title="Marmot">marmots</a>, in various areas, including <a href="/wiki/Central_Asia" title="Central Asia">Central Asia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kurdistan" title="Kurdistan">Kurdistan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Western_Asia" title="Western Asia">Western Asia</a>, <a href="/wiki/North_India" title="North India">North India</a>, <a href="/wiki/Uganda" title="Uganda">Uganda</a> and the western United States.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEZiegler199825_48-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEZiegler199825-48">[45]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[46]</a></sup>
</p><p><i>Y. pestis</i> was discovered by <a href="/wiki/Alexandre_Yersin" title="Alexandre Yersin">Alexandre Yersin</a>, a pupil of <a href="/wiki/Louis_Pasteur" title="Louis Pasteur">Louis Pasteur</a>, during an epidemic of bubonic plague in Hong Kong in 1894; Yersin also proved this <a href="/wiki/Bacilli" title="Bacilli">bacillus</a> was present in rodents and suggested the rat was the main vehicle of transmission.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEArrizabalaga2010_50-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEArrizabalaga2010-50">[47]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[48]</a></sup> The mechanism by which <i>Y. pestis</i> is usually transmitted was established in 1898 by <a href="/wiki/Paul-Louis_Simond" title="Paul-Louis Simond">Paul-Louis Simond</a> and was found to involve the bites of fleas whose <a href="/wiki/Midgut" title="Midgut">midguts</a> had become obstructed by replicating <i>Y. pestis</i> several days after feeding on an infected host. This blockage starves the fleas and drives them to aggressive feeding behaviour and attempts to clear the blockage by <a href="/wiki/Vomiting" title="Vomiting">regurgitation</a>, resulting in thousands of plague bacteria being flushed into the feeding site, infecting the host. The bubonic plague mechanism was also dependent on two populations of rodents: one resistant to the disease, which act as <a href="/wiki/Host_(biology)" title="Host (biology)">hosts</a>, keeping the disease <a href="/wiki/Endemic_(epidemiology)" title="Endemic (epidemiology)">endemic</a>, and a second that lacks resistance. When the second population dies, the fleas move on to other hosts, including people, thus creating a human <a href="/wiki/Epidemic_typhus" title="Epidemic typhus">epidemic</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChristakosOleaSerreWang2005110–14_29-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChristakosOleaSerreWang2005110–14-29">[27]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="DNA_evidence">DNA evidence</span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Bubonic_plague_victims-mass_grave_in_Martigues,_France_1720-1721.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="447" data-file-width="600" decoding="async" height="164" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/Bubonic_plague_victims-mass_grave_in_Martigues%2C_France_1720-1721.jpg/220px-Bubonic_plague_victims-mass_grave_in_Martigues%2C_France_1720-1721.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/Bubonic_plague_victims-mass_grave_in_Martigues%2C_France_1720-1721.jpg/330px-Bubonic_plague_victims-mass_grave_in_Martigues%2C_France_1720-1721.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/Bubonic_plague_victims-mass_grave_in_Martigues%2C_France_1720-1721.jpg/440px-Bubonic_plague_victims-mass_grave_in_Martigues%2C_France_1720-1721.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Bubonic_plague_victims-mass_grave_in_Martigues,_France_1720-1721.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Skeletons in a mass grave from 1720 to 1721 in <a href="/wiki/Martigues" title="Martigues">Martigues</a>, near <a href="/wiki/Marseille" title="Marseille">Marseille</a> in southern France, yielded molecular evidence of the <i>orientalis</i> strain of <i>Yersinia pestis</i>, the organism responsible for bubonic plague. The second pandemic of bubonic plague was active in Europe from 1347, the beginning of the Black Death, until 1750.</div></div></div>
<p>Definitive confirmation of the role of <i>Y. pestis</i> arrived in 2010 with a publication in <i><a href="/wiki/PLOS_Pathogens" title="PLOS Pathogens">PLOS Pathogens</a></i> by Haensch et al.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHaenschBianucciSignoliRajerison2010_5-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHaenschBianucciSignoliRajerison2010-5">[4]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[d]</a></sup> They assessed the presence of <a href="/wiki/DNA" title="DNA">DNA</a>/<a href="/wiki/RNA" title="RNA">RNA</a> with <a href="/wiki/Polymerase_chain_reaction" title="Polymerase chain reaction">polymerase chain reaction</a> (PCR) techniques for <i>Y. pestis</i> from the <a href="/wiki/Dental_alveolus" title="Dental alveolus">tooth sockets</a> in human skeletons from mass graves in northern, central and southern Europe that were associated archaeologically with the Black Death and subsequent resurgences. The authors concluded that this new research, together with prior analyses from the south of France and Germany, "ends the debate about the cause of the Black Death, and unambiguously demonstrates that <i>Y. pestis</i> was the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Disease_causative_agent" title="Disease causative agent">causative agent</a> of the epidemic plague that devastated Europe during the Middle Ages".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHaenschBianucciSignoliRajerison2010_5-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHaenschBianucciSignoliRajerison2010-5">[4]</a></sup> In 2011, these results were further confirmed with genetic evidence derived from Black Death victims in the <a href="/wiki/East_Smithfield" title="East Smithfield">East Smithfield</a> burial site in England. Schuenemann et al. concluded in 2011 "that the Black Death in medieval Europe was caused by a variant of <i>Y. pestis</i> that may no longer exist".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBos2011_55-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBos2011-55">[51]</a></sup>
</p><p>Later in 2011, <a href="/wiki/Kirsten_Bos" title="Kirsten Bos">Bos</a> et al. reported in <i><a href="/wiki/Nature_(journal)" title="Nature (journal)">Nature</a></i> the first draft genome of <i>Y. pestis</i> from plague victims from the same East Smithfield cemetery and indicated that the strain that caused the Black Death is ancestral to most modern strains of <i>Y. pestis</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBos2011_55-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBos2011-55">[51]</a></sup>
</p><p>Since this time, further genomic papers have further confirmed the <a href="/wiki/Phylogenetics" title="Phylogenetics">phylogenetic</a> placement of the <i>Y. pestis</i> strain responsible for the Black Death as both the ancestor<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESpyrouKellerTukhbatovaScheib2019_56-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESpyrouKellerTukhbatovaScheib2019-56">[52]</a></sup> of later plague epidemics including the <a href="/wiki/Third_plague_pandemic" title="Third plague pandemic">third plague pandemic</a> and as the descendant<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWagnerKlunkHarbeckDevault2014_57-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWagnerKlunkHarbeckDevault2014-57">[53]</a></sup> of the strain responsible for the <a href="/wiki/Plague_of_Justinian" title="Plague of Justinian">Plague of Justinian</a>. In addition, plague genomes from significantly earlier in prehistory have been recovered.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERasmussenAllentoftNielsenOrlando2015_58-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERasmussenAllentoftNielsenOrlando2015-58">[54]</a></sup>
</p><p>DNA taken from 25 skeletons from 14th century London have shown plague is a strain of <i>Y. pestis</i> almost identical to that which <a href="/wiki/21st_century_Madagascar_plague_outbreaks" title="21st century Madagascar plague outbreaks">hit Madagascar in 2013</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-guardian_59-0"><a href="#cite_note-guardian-59">[55]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[56]</a></sup> Further DNA evidence also proves the role of <i>Y.  Pestis</i> and traces the source to the <a href="/wiki/Tian_Shan" title="Tian Shan">Tian Shan</a> Mountains in <a href="/wiki/Kyrgyzstan" title="Kyrgyzstan">Kyrgyzstan</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[57]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Alternative_explanations">Alternative explanations</span></h4>
<p>It is recognised that an <a href="/wiki/Epidemiology" title="Epidemiology">epidemiological</a> account of plague is as important as an identification of symptoms, but researchers are hampered by the lack of reliable statistics from this period. Most work has been done on the spread of the disease in England, and even estimates of overall population at the start vary by over 100% as no census was undertaken in England between the time of publication of the <a href="/wiki/Domesday_Book" title="Domesday Book">Domesday Book</a> of 1086 and the <a href="/wiki/Poll_tax#Great_Britain" title="Poll tax">poll tax</a> of the year 1377.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEZiegler1998233_62-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEZiegler1998233-62">[58]</a></sup> Estimates of plague victims are usually <a href="/wiki/Extrapolation" title="Extrapolation">extrapolated</a> from figures for the clergy.
</p><p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mathematical_modelling" title="Mathematical modelling">Mathematical modelling</a> is used to match the spreading patterns and the means of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Transmission_(medicine)" title="Transmission (medicine)">transmission</a>. A research in 2018 challenged the popular hypothesis that "infected rats died, their flea parasites could have jumped from the recently dead rat hosts to humans". It suggested an alternative model in which "the disease was spread from human fleas and body lice to other people". The second model claims to better fit the trends of death toll because the rat-flea-human hypothesis would have produced a delayed but very high spike in deaths, which contradict historical death data.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[59]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[60]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Lars_Wall%C3%B8e" title="Lars Walløe">Lars Walløe</a> complains that all of these authors "take it for granted that Simond's infection model, black rat → rat flea → human, which was developed to explain the spread of plague in India, is the only way an epidemic of <i>Yersinia pestis</i> infection could spread", whilst pointing to several other possibilities.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWalløe200869_65-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWalløe200869-65">[61]</a></sup> Similarly, <a href="/wiki/Monica_Green_(historian)" title="Monica Green (historian)">Monica Green</a> has argued that greater attention is needed to the range of (especially non-<a href="/wiki/Commensalism" title="Commensalism">commensal</a>) animals that might be involved in the transmission of plague.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGreen201531ff_38-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGreen201531ff-38">[35]</a></sup>
</p><p>Archaeologist Barney Sloane has argued that there is insufficient evidence of the extinction of numerous rats in the archaeological record of the medieval waterfront in London and that the disease spread too quickly to support the thesis that <i>Y. pestis</i> was spread from fleas on rats; he argues that transmission must have been person to person.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[62]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESloane2011_67-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESloane2011-67">[63]</a></sup> This theory is supported by research in 2018 which suggested transmission was more likely by body lice and <a href="/wiki/Oriental_rat_flea" title="Oriental rat flea">fleas</a> during the <a href="/wiki/Second_plague_pandemic" title="Second plague pandemic">second plague pandemic</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDeanKrauerWalløeLingjærde2018_68-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDeanKrauerWalløeLingjærde2018-68">[64]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Summary">Summary</span></h4>
<p>Although academic debate continues, no single alternative solution has achieved widespread acceptance.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChristakosOleaSerreWang2005110–14_29-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChristakosOleaSerreWang2005110–14-29">[27]</a></sup> Many scholars arguing for <i>Y. pestis</i> as the major agent of the pandemic suggest that its extent and symptoms can be explained by a combination of bubonic plague with other diseases, including <a href="/wiki/Typhus" title="Typhus">typhus</a>, <a href="/wiki/Smallpox" title="Smallpox">smallpox</a> and <a href="/wiki/Respiratory_tract_infection" title="Respiratory tract infection">respiratory infections</a>. In addition to the bubonic infection, others point to additional septicaemic (a type of "blood poisoning") and pneumonic (an airborne plague that attacks the lungs before the rest of the body) forms of plague, which lengthen the duration of outbreaks throughout the seasons and help account for its high mortality rate and additional recorded symptoms.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Byrne2004pp21-9_69-0"><a href="#cite_note-Byrne2004pp21-9-69">[65]</a></sup> In 2014, <a href="/wiki/Public_Health_England" title="Public Health England">Public Health England</a> announced the results of an examination of 25 bodies exhumed in the <a href="/wiki/Clerkenwell" title="Clerkenwell">Clerkenwell</a> area of London, as well as of wills registered in London during the period, which supported the pneumonic hypothesis.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-guardian_59-1"><a href="#cite_note-guardian-59">[55]</a></sup> Currently, while <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Osteoarchaeology" title="Osteoarchaeology">osteoarcheologists</a> have conclusively verified the presence of <i>Y. pestis</i> bacteria in burial sites across northern Europe through examination of bones and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dental_pulp" title="Dental pulp">dental pulp</a>, no other epidemic pathogen has been discovered to bolster the alternative explanations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESnowden201950–51_70-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESnowden201950–51-70">[66]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Transmission">Transmission</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Black_Death_migration" title="Black Death migration">Black Death migration</a></div>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Lack_of_hygiene">Lack of hygiene</span></h4>
<p>The importance of <a href="/wiki/Hygiene" title="Hygiene">hygiene</a> was recognised only in the nineteenth century with the development of the <a href="/wiki/Germ_theory_of_disease" title="Germ theory of disease">germ theory of disease</a>; until then streets were commonly filthy, with live animals of all sorts around and human parasites abounding, facilitating the spread of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Transmission_(medicine)" title="Transmission (medicine)">transmissible disease</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[67]</a></sup>
</p><p>By the early fourteenth century so much filth had collected inside urban Europe that French and Italian cities were naming streets after human waste. In medieval Paris, several street names were inspired by merde, the French word for "shit". There were rue Merdeux, rue Merdelet, rue Merdusson, rue des Merdons, and rue Merdiere—as well as a rue du Pipi.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[68]</a></sup>
</p><p>Pigs, cattle, chickens, geese, goats, and horses roamed the streets of medieval London and Paris. Medieval homeowners were supposed to police their housefronts, including removing animal dung, but most urbanites were as careless as William E. Cosner, a resident of the London suburb of Farringdon Without. A complaint lodged against Cosner charges that "men could not pass [by his house] for the stink [of]. . . horse dung and horse piss."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[69]</a></sup>
</p><p>One irate Londoner complained that the runoff from the local slaughterhouse had made his garden "stinking and putrid", while another charged that the blood from slain animals flooded nearby streets and lanes, "making a foul corruption and abominable sight to all dwelling near." In much of medieval Europe, sanitation legislation consisted of an ordinance requiring homeowners to shout, "Look out below!" three times before dumping a full chamber pot into the street.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[70]</a></sup>
</p><p>Early Christians, who thought self-abnegation a cardinal virtue, considered bathing, if not a vice, then a temptation. "Who knows what impure thoughts might arise in a tub of warm water? With this danger in mind, St. Benedict declared, <i>"To those who are well, and especially to the young, bathing shall seldom be permitted."</i> St. Agnes took the injunction to heart and died without ever bathing."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[71]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Territorial_origins">Territorial origins</span></h4>
<p>According to a team of <a href="/wiki/Medical_genetics" title="Medical genetics">medical geneticists</a> led by <a href="/wiki/Mark_Achtman" title="Mark Achtman">Mark Achtman</a> that analysed the <a href="/wiki/Genetic_variation" title="Genetic variation">genetic variation</a> of the bacterium, <i>Yersinia pestis</i> "evolved in or near China" over 2,600 years ago.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[72]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[73]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMorelliSongMazzoniEppinger2010_78-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMorelliSongMazzoniEppinger2010-78">[74]</a></sup> Later research by a team led by Galina Eroshenko places the origins more specifically in the <a href="/wiki/Tian_Shan" title="Tian Shan">Tian Shan</a> mountains on the border between <a href="/wiki/Kyrgyzstan" title="Kyrgyzstan">Kyrgyzstan</a> and China.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid29073248_79-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid29073248-79">[75]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESlavin2019_80-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESlavin2019-80">[76]</a></sup> However more recent research notes that the previous sampling had a very heavy East Asian bias and that sampling since then has discovered strains of <i>Y. pestis</i> in the Caucasus region that were previously thought to be restricted to China.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-spyrou_81-0"><a href="#cite_note-spyrou-81">[77]</a></sup> There is also no physical or specific textual evidence of the Black Death in 14th century China. As a result, China's place in the sequence of the plague's spread is still debated to this day.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-telegraph_china_82-0"><a href="#cite_note-telegraph_china-82">[78]</a></sup> According to Charles Creighton, records of epidemics in 14th century China suggest nothing more than typhus and major Chinese outbreaks of epidemic disease post-date the European epidemic by several years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[79]</a></sup> The earliest Chinese descriptions of the bubonic plague do not appear until the 1640s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESussman2011_84-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESussman2011-84">[80]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Nestorianism" title="Nestorianism">Nestorian</a> graves dating to 1338–1339 near <a href="/wiki/Issyk-Kul" title="Issyk-Kul">Issyk-Kul</a> in Kyrgyzstan have inscriptions referring to plague, which has led some historians and <a href="/wiki/Epidemiology" title="Epidemiology">epidemiologists</a> to think they mark the outbreak of the <a href="/wiki/Epidemic" title="Epidemic">epidemic</a>; this is supported by recent direct findings of <i>Y. pestis</i> DNA in teeth samples from graves in the area with inscriptions referring to "pestilence" as the cause of death.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Spyrou_1–7_11-1"><a href="#cite_note-Spyrou_1–7-11">[10]</a></sup> Epidemics killed an estimated 25 million across Asia during the fifteen years before the Black Death reached <a href="/wiki/Constantinople" title="Constantinople">Constantinople</a> in 1347.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[81]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[82]</a></sup>
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r996844942">.mw-parser-output .templatequote{overflow:hidden;margin:1em 0;padding:0 40px}.mw-parser-output .templatequote .templatequotecite{line-height:1.5em;text-align:left;padding-left:1.6em;margin-top:0}</style><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>The evidence does not suggest, at least at present, that these mortality crises were caused by plague. Although some scholars, including McNeill and Cao, see the 1333 outbreak as a prelude to the outbreaks in Europe from the late 1340s to the early 1350s, scholars of the Yuan and Ming periods remain skeptical about such an interpretation. Nonetheless, the remarkably high mortality rates during the Datong mortality should discourage us from rejecting the possibility of localized/regional outbreaks of plague in different parts of China, albeit differing in scale from, and unrelated to, the pandemic mortality of the Black Death. What we lack is any indication of a plague pandemic that engulfed vast territories of the Yuan Empire and later moved into western Eurasia through Central Asia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESlavin2019_80-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESlavin2019-80">[76]</a></sup></p><div class="templatequotecite">— <cite>Philip Slavin</cite></div></blockquote>
<p>According to John Norris, evidence from Issyk-Kul indicates a small sporadic outbreak characteristic of transmission from rodents to humans with no wide-scale impact.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESussman2011_84-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESussman2011-84">[80]</a></sup>  According to Achtman, the dating of the plague suggests that it was not carried along the <a href="/wiki/Silk_Road" title="Silk Road">Silk Road</a>, and its widespread appearance in that region probably postdates the European outbreak.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-telegraph_china_82-1"><a href="#cite_note-telegraph_china-82">[78]</a></sup> There are no records of the symptoms of the Black Death from Mongol sources or writings from travelers east of the <a href="/wiki/Black_Sea" title="Black Sea">Black Sea</a> prior to the Crimean outbreak in 1346. Finally, the Silk Road had already been heavily disrupted before the spread of the Black Death. Western and Middle Eastern traders found it difficult to trade on the Silk Road by 1325 and impossible by 1340, making spread of the plague less likely.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESussman2011_84-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESussman2011-84">[80]</a></sup>
</p><p>Others still favor an origin in China<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESlavin2019_80-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESlavin2019-80">[76]</a></sup> or even Kurdistan, and not Central Asia. According to the theory of Chinese origin, the disease may have traveled along the <a href="/wiki/Silk_Road" title="Silk Road">Silk Road</a> with <a href="/wiki/Mongols" title="Mongols">Mongol</a> armies and traders, or it could have arrived via ship,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-telegraph_china_82-2"><a href="#cite_note-telegraph_china-82">[78]</a></sup> but this theory is still contested. It is speculated that rats aboard <a href="/wiki/Zheng_He" title="Zheng He">Zheng He</a>'s ships in the 15th century may have carried the plague to <a href="/wiki/Southeast_Asia" title="Southeast Asia">Southeast Asia</a>, <a href="/wiki/India" title="India">India</a>, and Africa.
</p><p>Research on the <a href="/wiki/Delhi_Sultanate" title="Delhi Sultanate">Delhi Sultanate</a> and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Yuan_Dynasty" title="Yuan Dynasty">Yuan Dynasty</a> shows no evidence of any serious epidemic in fourteenth-century India and no specific evidence of plague in fourteenth-century China, suggesting that the Black Death may not have reached these regions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESussman2011_84-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESussman2011-84">[80]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-telegraph_china_82-3"><a href="#cite_note-telegraph_china-82">[78]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBenedictow200448–49_87-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBenedictow200448–49-87">[83]</a></sup> Ole Benedictow argues that since the first clear reports of the Black Death come from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kaffa_(city)" title="Kaffa (city)">Kaffa</a>, the Black Death most likely originated in the nearby plague focus on the northwestern shore of the <a href="/wiki/Caspian_Sea" title="Caspian Sea">Caspian Sea</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBenedictow200450–51_88-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBenedictow200450–51-88">[84]</a></sup>
</p>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r996844942" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>Demographic historians estimate that China's population fell by at least 15 percent, and perhaps as much as a third, between 1340 and 1370. This population loss coincided with the Black Death that ravaged Europe and much of the Islamic world in 1347–52. However, there is a conspicuous lack of evidence for pandemic disease on the scale of the Black Death in China at this time. War and famine – and the diseases that typically accompanied them – probably were the main causes of mortality in the final decades of Mongol rule.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEvon_Glahn2016440_89-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEvon_Glahn2016440-89">[85]</a></sup></p><div class="templatequotecite">— <cite>Richard von Glahn</cite></div></blockquote>
<p>Monica H. Green suggests that the reason why other parts of <a href="/wiki/Eurasia" title="Eurasia">Eurasia</a> outside the west do not contain the same evidence of the Black Plague is because there were actually four strains of <i>Yersinia pestis</i> that became predominant in different parts of the world. Mongol records of illness such as food poisoning may have been referring to the Black Plague.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGreen2020_90-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGreen2020-90">[86]</a></sup> Another theory is that the Black Death originated near Europe and cycled through the Mediterranean, Northern Europe, and Russia before making its way to China.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-spyrou_81-1"><a href="#cite_note-spyrou-81">[77]</a></sup> Other historians such as John Norris and Ole Benedictaw believe the Black Death likely originated in Europe or the Middle East and never reached China.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESussman2011354_91-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESussman2011354-91">[87]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="European_outbreak">European outbreak</span></h4>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1062260506">.mw-parser-output .quotebox{background-color:#F9F9F9;border:1px solid #aaa;box-sizing:border-box;padding:10px;font-size:88%;max-width:100%}.mw-parser-output .quotebox.floatleft{margin:.5em 1.4em .8em 0}.mw-parser-output .quotebox.floatright{margin:.5em 0 .8em 1.4em}.mw-parser-output .quotebox.centered{overflow:hidden;position:relative;margin:.5em auto .8em auto}.mw-parser-output .quotebox.floatleft span,.mw-parser-output .quotebox.floatright span{font-style:inherit}.mw-parser-output .quotebox>blockquote{margin:0;padding:0;border-left:0;font-family:inherit;font-size:inherit}.mw-parser-output .quotebox-title{background-color:#F9F9F9;text-align:center;font-size:110%;font-weight:bold}.mw-parser-output .quotebox-quote>:first-child{margin-top:0}.mw-parser-output .quotebox-quote:last-child>:last-child{margin-bottom:0}.mw-parser-output .quotebox-quote.quoted:before{font-family:"Times New Roman",serif;font-weight:bold;font-size:large;color:gray;content:" “ ";vertical-align:-45%;line-height:0}.mw-parser-output .quotebox-quote.quoted:after{font-family:"Times New Roman",serif;font-weight:bold;font-size:large;color:gray;content:" ” ";line-height:0}.mw-parser-output .quotebox .left-aligned{text-align:left}.mw-parser-output .quotebox .right-aligned{text-align:right}.mw-parser-output .quotebox .center-aligned{text-align:center}.mw-parser-output .quotebox .quote-title,.mw-parser-output .quotebox .quotebox-quote{display:block}.mw-parser-output .quotebox cite{display:block;font-style:normal}@media screen and (max-width:640px){.mw-parser-output .quotebox{width:100%!important;margin:0 0 .8em!important;float:none!important}}</style><div class="quotebox pullquote floatright" style="width:25em; ;">
<blockquote class="quotebox-quote left-aligned" style=""><p>
The seventh year after it began, it came to England and first began in the towns and ports joining on the seacoasts, in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dorsetshire" title="Dorsetshire">Dorsetshire</a>, where, as in other counties, it made the country quite void of inhabitants so that there were almost none left alive.</p><p>... But at length it came to <a href="/wiki/Gloucester" title="Gloucester">Gloucester</a>, yea even to <a href="/wiki/Oxford" title="Oxford">Oxford</a> and to London, and finally it spread over all England and so wasted the people that scarce the tenth person of any sort was left alive.</p>
</blockquote>
<p><cite class="left-aligned" style=""><a href="/wiki/Geoffrey_the_Baker" title="Geoffrey the Baker">Geoffrey the Baker</a>, <i>Chronicon Angliae</i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92"><a href="#cite_note-92">[88]</a></sup></cite>
</p>
</div>
<p>Plague was reportedly first introduced to Europe via <a href="/wiki/Republic_of_Genoa" title="Republic of Genoa">Genoese</a> traders from their port city of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kaffa_(city)" title="Kaffa (city)">Kaffa</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Crimea" title="Crimea">Crimea</a> in 1347. During a <a href="/wiki/Genoese%E2%80%93Mongol_Wars" title="Genoese–Mongol Wars">protracted siege</a> of the city, in 1345–1346 the Mongol <a href="/wiki/Golden_Horde" title="Golden Horde">Golden Horde</a> army of <a href="/wiki/Jani_Beg" title="Jani Beg">Jani Beg</a>, whose mainly <a href="/wiki/Tatars" title="Tatars">Tatar</a> troops were suffering from the disease, <a href="/wiki/Biological_warfare" title="Biological warfare">catapulted infected corpses</a> over the city walls of Kaffa to infect the inhabitants,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWheelis2002_93-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWheelis2002-93">[89]</a></sup> though it is more likely that infected rats travelled across the siege lines to spread the epidemic to the inhabitants.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBarrasGreub2014_94-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBarrasGreub2014-94">[90]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95"><a href="#cite_note-95">[91]</a></sup> As the disease took hold, Genoese traders fled across the <a href="/wiki/Black_Sea" title="Black Sea">Black Sea</a> to <a href="/wiki/Constantinople" title="Constantinople">Constantinople</a>, where the disease first arrived in Europe in summer 1347.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:5_96-0"><a href="#cite_note-:5-96">[92]</a></sup>
</p><p>The epidemic there killed the 13-year-old son of the <a href="/wiki/Byzantine_Empire" title="Byzantine Empire">Byzantine emperor</a>, <a href="/wiki/John_VI_Kantakouzenos" title="John VI Kantakouzenos">John VI Kantakouzenos</a>, who wrote a description of the disease modelled on <a href="/wiki/Thucydides" title="Thucydides">Thucydides</a>'s account of the 5th century BCE <a href="/wiki/Plague_of_Athens" title="Plague of Athens">Plague of Athens</a>, but noting the spread of the Black Death by ship between maritime cities.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:5_96-1"><a href="#cite_note-:5-96">[92]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Nicephorus_Gregoras" title="Nicephorus Gregoras">Nicephorus Gregoras</a> also described in writing to <a href="/wiki/Demetrios_Kydones" title="Demetrios Kydones">Demetrios Kydones</a> the rising death toll, the futility of medicine, and the panic of the citizens.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:5_96-2"><a href="#cite_note-:5-96">[92]</a></sup> The first outbreak in Constantinople lasted a year, but the disease recurred ten times before 1400.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:5_96-3"><a href="#cite_note-:5-96">[92]</a></sup>
</p><p>Carried by twelve Genoese galleys, plague arrived by ship in <a href="/wiki/Sicily" title="Sicily">Sicily</a> in October 1347;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97"><a href="#cite_note-97">[93]</a></sup> the disease spread rapidly all over the island. Galleys from Kaffa reached Genoa and Venice in January 1348, but it was the outbreak in <a href="/wiki/Pisa" title="Pisa">Pisa</a> a few weeks later that was the entry point to northern Italy. Towards the end of January, one of the galleys expelled from Italy arrived in <a href="/wiki/Marseille" title="Marseille">Marseilles</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98"><a href="#cite_note-98">[94]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Black_Death_in_Italy" title="Black Death in Italy">From Italy</a>, the disease spread northwest across Europe, <a href="/wiki/Black_Death_in_France" title="Black Death in France">striking France</a>, <a href="/wiki/Black_Death_in_Spain" title="Black Death in Spain">Spain</a> (the epidemic began to wreak havoc first on the <a href="/wiki/Crown_of_Aragon" title="Crown of Aragon">Crown of Aragon</a> in the spring of 1348),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99"><a href="#cite_note-99">[95]</a></sup> Portugal and <a href="/wiki/Black_Death_in_England" title="Black Death in England">England</a> by June 1348, then spread east and north <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Black_Death_in_Germany" title="Black Death in Germany">through Germany</a>, Scotland and Scandinavia from 1348 to 1350. It was introduced <a href="/wiki/Black_Death_in_Norway" title="Black Death in Norway">into Norway</a> in 1349 when a ship landed at <a href="/wiki/Ask%C3%B8y" title="Askøy">Askøy</a>, then spread to Bjørgvin (modern <a href="/wiki/Bergen" title="Bergen">Bergen</a>) and <a href="/wiki/Iceland" title="Iceland">Iceland</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKarlsson2000111_100-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKarlsson2000111-100">[96]</a></sup> Finally, it spread to northwestern Russia in 1351. Plague was somewhat more uncommon in parts of Europe with less developed trade with their neighbours, including the majority of the <a href="/wiki/Basque_Country_(greater_region)" title="Basque Country (greater region)">Basque Country</a>, isolated parts of Belgium and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Black_Death_in_the_Netherlands" title="Black Death in the Netherlands">the Netherlands</a>, and isolated Alpine villages throughout the continent.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEZuchora-Walske2013_101-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEZuchora-Walske2013-101">[97]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWelfordBossak2010_102-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWelfordBossak2010-102">[98]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103"><a href="#cite_note-103">[99]</a></sup>
</p><p>According to some epidemiologists, periods of unfavourable weather decimated plague-infected rodent populations and forced their fleas onto alternative hosts,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESamiaKausrudHeesterbeekAgeyev2011_104-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESamiaKausrudHeesterbeekAgeyev2011-104">[100]</a></sup> inducing plague outbreaks which often peaked in the hot summers of the Mediterranean,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECohn2008_105-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECohn2008-105">[101]</a></sup> as well as during the cool autumn months of the southern <a href="/wiki/Baltic_region" title="Baltic region">Baltic region</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-106"><a href="#cite_note-106">[102]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-109"><a href="#cite_note-109">[e]</a></sup> Among many other culprits of plague contagiousness, malnutrition, even if distantly, also contributed to such an immense loss in European population, since it weakened immune systems.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Baten_110-0"><a href="#cite_note-Baten-110">[105]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Western_Asian_and_North_African_outbreak">Western Asian and North African outbreak</span></h4>
<p>The disease struck various regions in the Middle East and North Africa during the <a href="/wiki/Pandemic" title="Pandemic">pandemic</a>, leading to serious depopulation and permanent change in both economic and social structures.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGreen2018_111-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGreen2018-111">[106]</a></sup> As infected rodents infected new rodents, the disease spread across the region, entering also from southern Russia.
</p><p>By autumn 1347, plague had reached <a href="/wiki/Alexandria" title="Alexandria">Alexandria</a> in Egypt, transmitted by sea from <a href="/wiki/Constantinople" title="Constantinople">Constantinople</a>; according to a contemporary witness, from a single merchant ship carrying slaves.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:6_112-0"><a href="#cite_note-:6-112">[107]</a></sup> By late summer 1348 it reached Cairo, capital of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mamluk_Sultanate_(Cairo)" title="Mamluk Sultanate (Cairo)">Mamluk Sultanate</a>, cultural centre of the <a href="/wiki/Muslim_world" title="Muslim world">Islamic world</a>, and the largest city in the <a href="/wiki/Mediterranean_Basin" title="Mediterranean Basin">Mediterranean Basin</a>; the <a href="/wiki/Bahri_dynasty" title="Bahri dynasty">Bahriyya</a> child sultan <a href="/wiki/An-Nasir_Hasan" title="An-Nasir Hasan">an-Nasir Hasan</a> fled and more than a third of the 600,000 residents died.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:7_113-0"><a href="#cite_note-:7-113">[108]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Nile" title="Nile">Nile</a> was choked with corpses despite Cairo having a medieval hospital, the late 13th century <a href="/wiki/Bimaristan" title="Bimaristan">bimaristan</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Qalawun_complex" title="Qalawun complex">Qalawun complex</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:7_113-1"><a href="#cite_note-:7-113">[108]</a></sup> The historian <a href="/wiki/Al-Maqrizi" title="Al-Maqrizi">al-Maqrizi</a> described the abundant work for grave-diggers and practitioners of <a href="/wiki/Islamic_funeral" title="Islamic funeral">funeral rites</a>, and plague recurred in Cairo more than fifty times over the following one and a half centuries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:7_113-2"><a href="#cite_note-:7-113">[108]</a></sup>
</p><p>During 1347, the disease travelled eastward to <a href="/wiki/Gaza_City" title="Gaza City">Gaza</a> by April; by July it had reached <a href="/wiki/Damascus" title="Damascus">Damascus</a>, and in October plague had broken out in <a href="/wiki/Aleppo" title="Aleppo">Aleppo</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:6_112-1"><a href="#cite_note-:6-112">[107]</a></sup> That year, in <a href="/wiki/Syria_(region)" title="Syria (region)">the territory</a> of modern <a href="/wiki/Lebanon" title="Lebanon">Lebanon</a>, <a href="/wiki/Syria" title="Syria">Syria</a>, <a href="/wiki/Israel" title="Israel">Israel</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Palestinian_territories" title="Palestinian territories">Palestine</a>, the cities of <a href="/wiki/Ashkelon" title="Ashkelon">Ashkelon</a>, <a href="/wiki/Acre,_Israel" title="Acre, Israel">Acre</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jerusalem" title="Jerusalem">Jerusalem</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sidon" title="Sidon">Sidon</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Homs" title="Homs">Homs</a> were all infected. In 1348–1349, the disease reached <a href="/wiki/Antioch" title="Antioch">Antioch</a>. The city's residents fled to the north, but most of them ended up dying during the journey.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-114"><a href="#cite_note-114">[109]</a></sup> Within two years, the plague had spread throughout the Islamic world, from Arabia across North Africa.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKelly2005_44-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKelly2005-44">[41]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources"><span title="This citation requires a reference to the specific page or range of pages in which the material appears. (May 2021)">page needed</span></a></i>]</sup> The pandemic spread westwards from Alexandria along the African coast, while in April 1348 <a href="/wiki/Tunis" title="Tunis">Tunis</a> was infected by ship from Sicily. Tunis was then under attack by an army from Morocco; this army dispersed in 1348 and brought the contagion with them to Morocco, whose epidemic may also have been seeded from the Islamic city of <a href="/wiki/Almer%C3%ADa" title="Almería">Almería</a> in <a href="/wiki/Al-Andalus" title="Al-Andalus">al-Andalus</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:6_112-2"><a href="#cite_note-:6-112">[107]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Mecca" title="Mecca">Mecca</a> became infected in 1348 by pilgrims performing the <a href="/wiki/Hajj" title="Hajj">Hajj</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:6_112-3"><a href="#cite_note-:6-112">[107]</a></sup> In 1351 or 1352, the <a href="/wiki/Rasulid_dynasty" title="Rasulid dynasty">Rasulid</a> sultan of the <a href="/wiki/Yemen" title="Yemen">Yemen</a>, <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Al-Mujahid_Ali&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Al-Mujahid Ali (page does not exist)">al-Mujahid Ali</a>, was released from Mamluk captivity in Egypt and carried plague with him on his return home.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:6_112-4"><a href="#cite_note-:6-112">[107]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-115"><a href="#cite_note-115">[110]</a></sup> During 1348, records show the city of <a href="/wiki/Mosul" title="Mosul">Mosul</a> suffered a massive epidemic, and the city of <a href="/wiki/Baghdad" title="Baghdad">Baghdad</a> experienced a second round of the disease.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (April 2020)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Signs_and_symptoms">Signs and symptoms</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Acral_gangrene_due_to_plague.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2144" data-file-width="3231" decoding="async" height="146" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/88/Acral_gangrene_due_to_plague.jpg/220px-Acral_gangrene_due_to_plague.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/88/Acral_gangrene_due_to_plague.jpg/330px-Acral_gangrene_due_to_plague.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/88/Acral_gangrene_due_to_plague.jpg/440px-Acral_gangrene_due_to_plague.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Acral_gangrene_due_to_plague.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A hand showing how <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/acral" title="wikt:acral">acral</a> <a href="/wiki/Gangrene" title="Gangrene">gangrene</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Digit_(anatomy)" title="Digit (anatomy)">fingers</a> due to <a href="/wiki/Bubonic_plague" title="Bubonic plague">bubonic plague</a> causes the skin and <a href="/wiki/Tissue_(biology)" title="Tissue (biology)">flesh</a> to <a href="/wiki/Necrosis" title="Necrosis">die</a> and turn black</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Plague_bubo.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="184" data-file-width="278" decoding="async" height="146" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5e/Plague_bubo.jpg/220px-Plague_bubo.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/5/5e/Plague_bubo.jpg 1.5x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Plague_bubo.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>An inguinal <a href="/wiki/Bubo" title="Bubo">bubo</a> on the upper thigh of a person infected with bubonic plague. Swollen <a href="/wiki/Lymph_node" title="Lymph node">lymph nodes</a> (<i>buboes</i>) often occur in the neck, armpit and groin (<i>inguinal</i>) regions of plague victims.</div></div></div>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Bubonic_plague">Bubonic plague</span></h4>
<p>Symptoms of the disease include fever of 38–41 °C (100–106 °F), headaches, <a href="/wiki/Arthralgia" title="Arthralgia">painful aching joints</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nausea" title="Nausea">nausea</a> and vomiting, and a general feeling of <a href="/wiki/Malaise" title="Malaise">malaise</a>. Left untreated, of those that contract the bubonic plague, 80 percent die within eight days.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-116"><a href="#cite_note-116">[111]</a></sup>
</p><p>Contemporary accounts of the pandemic are varied and often imprecise. The most commonly noted symptom was the appearance of <a href="/wiki/Bubo" title="Bubo">buboes</a> (or <i>gavocciolos</i>) in the groin, neck, and armpits, which oozed pus and bled when opened.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Byrne2004pp21-9_69-1"><a href="#cite_note-Byrne2004pp21-9-69">[65]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Giovanni_Boccaccio" title="Giovanni Boccaccio">Boccaccio</a>'s description:
</p>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r996844942" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><blockquote class="templatequote" style="font-size:100%"><p>In men and women alike it first betrayed itself by the emergence of certain <a href="/wiki/Neoplasm" title="Neoplasm">tumours</a> in the groin or armpits, some of which grew as large as a common apple, others as an egg ... From the two said parts of the body this deadly <i>gavocciolo</i> soon began to propagate and spread itself in all directions indifferently; after which the form of the <a href="/wiki/Disease" title="Disease">malady</a> began to change, black spots or livid making their appearance in many cases on the arm or the thigh or elsewhere, now few and large, now minute and numerous. As the <i>gavocciolo</i> had been and still was an infallible token of approaching death, such also were these spots on whomsoever they showed themselves.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-117"><a href="#cite_note-117">[112]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-118"><a href="#cite_note-118">[113]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-120"><a href="#cite_note-120">[f]</a></sup></p></blockquote>
<p>This was followed by acute <a href="/wiki/Fever" title="Fever">fever</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hematemesis" title="Hematemesis">vomiting of blood</a>. Most people died two to seven days after initial infection. Freckle-like spots and rashes,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-121"><a href="#cite_note-121">[115]</a></sup> which could have been caused by <a href="/wiki/Pulicosis" title="Pulicosis">flea-bites</a>, were identified as another potential sign of plague.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Pneumonic_plague">Pneumonic plague</span></h4>
<p><a href="/wiki/Lodewijk_Heyligen" title="Lodewijk Heyligen">Lodewijk Heyligen</a>, whose master the <a href="/wiki/Giovanni_Colonna_(cardinal,_1295%E2%80%931348)" title="Giovanni Colonna (cardinal, 1295–1348)">Cardinal Colonna</a> died of plague in 1348, noted a distinct form of the disease, <a href="/wiki/Pneumonic_plague" title="Pneumonic plague">pneumonic plague</a>, that infected the lungs and led to respiratory problems.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Byrne2004pp21-9_69-2"><a href="#cite_note-Byrne2004pp21-9-69">[65]</a></sup> Symptoms include fever, cough, and <a href="/wiki/Hemoptysis" title="Hemoptysis">blood-tinged sputum</a>. As the disease progresses, sputum becomes free-flowing and bright red. Pneumonic plague has a mortality rate of 90 to 95 percent.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEByrne20048_122-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEByrne20048-122">[116]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Septicaemic_plague">Septicaemic plague</span></h4>
<p><a href="/wiki/Septicemic_plague" title="Septicemic plague">Septicaemic plague</a> is the least common of the three forms, with a mortality rate near 100%. Symptoms are high fevers and purple skin patches (<a href="/wiki/Purpura" title="Purpura">purpura</a> due to <a href="/wiki/Disseminated_intravascular_coagulation" title="Disseminated intravascular coagulation">disseminated intravascular coagulation</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEByrne20048_122-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEByrne20048-122">[116]</a></sup> In cases of pneumonic and particularly septicaemic plague, the progress of the disease is so rapid that there would often be no time for the development of the enlarged lymph nodes that were noted as buboes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEByrne20048_122-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEByrne20048-122">[116]</a></sup>
</p>
