<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Consequences">Consequences</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Consequences_of_the_Black_Death" title="Consequences of the Black Death">Consequences of the Black Death</a></div>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Deaths">Deaths</span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Nuremberg_chronicles_-_Dance_of_Death_(CCLXIIIIv).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1769" data-file-width="2074" decoding="async" height="188" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a7/Nuremberg_chronicles_-_Dance_of_Death_%28CCLXIIIIv%29.jpg/220px-Nuremberg_chronicles_-_Dance_of_Death_%28CCLXIIIIv%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a7/Nuremberg_chronicles_-_Dance_of_Death_%28CCLXIIIIv%29.jpg/330px-Nuremberg_chronicles_-_Dance_of_Death_%28CCLXIIIIv%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a7/Nuremberg_chronicles_-_Dance_of_Death_%28CCLXIIIIv%29.jpg/440px-Nuremberg_chronicles_-_Dance_of_Death_%28CCLXIIIIv%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Nuremberg_chronicles_-_Dance_of_Death_(CCLXIIIIv).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Inspired by the Black Death, <i>The Dance of Death</i>, or <i><a href="/wiki/Danse_Macabre" title="Danse Macabre">Danse Macabre</a></i>, an <a href="/wiki/Allegory" title="Allegory">allegory</a> on the universality of death, was a common painting motif in the late medieval period.</div></div></div>
<p>There are no exact figures for the death toll; the rate varied widely by locality. In urban centres, the greater the population before the outbreak, the longer the duration of the period of abnormal mortality.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-123"><a href="#cite_note-123">[117]</a></sup> It killed some <span class="nowrap">75 to 200 million</span> people in Eurasia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ABC/Reuters_124-0"><a href="#cite_note-ABC/Reuters-124">[118]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Black_Death's_Gene_Code_Cracked_125-0"><a href="#cite_note-Black_Death's_Gene_Code_Cracked-125">[119]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-De-coding_the_Black_Death_126-0"><a href="#cite_note-De-coding_the_Black_Death-126">[120]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template noprint noexcerpt Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wikipedia:NOTRS" title="Wikipedia:NOTRS"><span title="200 million number seems high, see talk page (April 2020)">better source needed</span></a></i>]</sup> A study published in 2022 of pollen samples across Europe between 1250 and 1450 was used to estimate changes in agricultural output before and after the Black Death. The authors found great variability in different regions, with evidence for high mortality in areas of Scandinavia, France, western Germany, Greece and central Italy, but uninterrupted agricultural growth in central and eastern Europe, Iberia and Ireland.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-127"><a href="#cite_note-127">[121]</a></sup>
</p><p>The mortality rate of the Black Death in the 14th century was far greater than the worst 20th-century outbreaks of <i>Y. pestis</i> plague, which occurred in India and killed as much as 3% of the population of certain cities.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cohn_2010_128-0"><a href="#cite_note-Cohn_2010-128">[122]</a></sup> The overwhelming number of deceased bodies produced by the Black Death caused the necessity of mass burial sites in Europe, sometimes including up to several hundred or several thousand skeletons.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:3_129-0"><a href="#cite_note-:3-129">[123]</a></sup> The mass burial sites that have been excavated have allowed archaeologists to continue interpreting and defining the biological, sociological, historical, and anthropological implications of the Black Death.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:3_129-1"><a href="#cite_note-:3-129">[123]</a></sup>
</p><p>According to medieval historian <a href="/wiki/Philip_Daileader" title="Philip Daileader">Philip Daileader</a>, it is likely that over four years, 45–50% of the European population died of plague.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Daileader_130-0"><a href="#cite_note-Daileader-130">[124]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-131"><a href="#cite_note-131">[g]</a></sup> Norwegian historian <a href="/wiki/Ole_J%C3%B8rgen_Benedictow" title="Ole Jørgen Benedictow">Ole Benedictow</a> suggests it could have been as much as 60% of the European population.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Benedictow_132-0"><a href="#cite_note-Benedictow-132">[125]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-133"><a href="#cite_note-133">[h]</a></sup> In 1348, the disease spread so rapidly that before any physicians or government authorities had time to reflect upon its origins, about a third of the European population had already perished. In crowded cities, it was not uncommon for as much as 50% of the population to die.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChristakosOleaSerreWang2005110–14_29-4"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChristakosOleaSerreWang2005110–14-29">[27]</a></sup> Half of Paris' population of 100,000 people died. In Italy, the population of <a href="/wiki/Florence" title="Florence">Florence</a> was reduced from between 110,000 and 120,000 inhabitants in 1338 down to 50,000 in 1351. At least 60% of the population of <a href="/wiki/Hamburg" title="Hamburg">Hamburg</a> and <a href="/wiki/Bremen" title="Bremen">Bremen</a> perished,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-134"><a href="#cite_note-134">[126]</a></sup> and a similar percentage of Londoners may have died from the disease as well,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-guardian_59-2"><a href="#cite_note-guardian-59">[55]</a></sup> with a death toll of approximately 62,000 between 1346 and 1353.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETignorBrownLiuShaw2014407_47-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETignorBrownLiuShaw2014407-47">[44]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-136"><a href="#cite_note-136">[i]</a></sup> Florence's tax records suggest that 80% of the city's population died within four months in 1348.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cohn_2010_128-1"><a href="#cite_note-Cohn_2010-128">[122]</a></sup> Before 1350, there were about 170,000 settlements in Germany, and this was reduced by nearly 40,000 by 1450.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-137"><a href="#cite_note-137">[128]</a></sup> The disease bypassed some areas, with the most isolated areas being less vulnerable to <a href="/wiki/Contagious_disease" title="Contagious disease">contagion</a>. Plague did not appear in <a href="/wiki/Douai" title="Douai">Douai</a> in <a href="/wiki/French_Flanders" title="French Flanders">Flanders</a> until the turn of the 15th century, and the impact was less severe on the populations of <a href="/wiki/County_of_Hainaut" title="County of Hainaut">Hainaut</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Medieval_Finland" title="Medieval Finland">Finland</a>, northern Germany, and areas of Poland.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cohn_2010_128-2"><a href="#cite_note-Cohn_2010-128">[122]</a></sup> Monks, nuns, and priests were especially hard-hit since they cared for people with  the Black Death.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBennettHollister2006329_138-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBennettHollister2006329-138">[129]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:292px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Doutielt3.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="482" data-file-width="801" decoding="async" height="175" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/55/Doutielt3.jpg/290px-Doutielt3.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/55/Doutielt3.jpg/435px-Doutielt3.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/55/Doutielt3.jpg/580px-Doutielt3.jpg 2x" width="290"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Doutielt3.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Citizens of <a href="/wiki/Tournai" title="Tournai">Tournai</a> bury plague victims</div></div></div>
<p>The physician to the <a href="/wiki/Avignon_Papacy" title="Avignon Papacy">Avignon Papacy</a>, Raimundo Chalmel de Vinario (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Latin_language" title="Latin language">Latin</a>: <i lang="la">Magister Raimundus</i>, <small><a href="/wiki/Literal_translation" title="Literal translation">lit.</a> </small>'Master Raymond'), observed the decreasing mortality rate of successive outbreaks of plague in 1347–48, 1362, 1371, and 1382 in his 1382 treatise <i>On Epidemics</i> (<span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">De epidemica</i></span>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:4_139-0"><a href="#cite_note-:4-139">[130]</a></sup> In the first outbreak, two thirds of the population contracted the illness and most patients died; in the next, half the population became ill but only some died; by the third, a tenth were affected and many survived; while by the fourth occurrence, only one in twenty people were sickened and most of them survived.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:4_139-1"><a href="#cite_note-:4-139">[130]</a></sup> By the 1380s in Europe, it predominantly affected children.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cohn_2010_128-3"><a href="#cite_note-Cohn_2010-128">[122]</a></sup> Chalmel de Vinario recognized that <a href="/wiki/Bloodletting" title="Bloodletting">bloodletting</a> was ineffective (though he continued to prescribe bleeding for members of the <a href="/wiki/Roman_Curia" title="Roman Curia">Roman Curia</a>, whom he disliked), and claimed that all true cases of plague were caused by <a href="/wiki/Medical_astrology" title="Medical astrology">astrological factors</a> and were incurable; he himself was never able to effect a cure.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:4_139-2"><a href="#cite_note-:4-139">[130]</a></sup>
</p><p>The most widely accepted estimate for the Middle East, including Iraq, Iran, and Syria, during this time, is for a death toll of about a third of the population.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-140"><a href="#cite_note-140">[131]</a></sup> The Black Death killed about 40% of Egypt's population.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-141"><a href="#cite_note-141">[132]</a></sup> In Cairo, with a population numbering as many as 600,000, and possibly the largest city west of China, between one third and 40% of the inhabitants died inside of eight months.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:7_113-3"><a href="#cite_note-:7-113">[108]</a></sup>
</p><p>Italian chronicler <a href="/wiki/Agnolo_di_Tura" title="Agnolo di Tura">Agnolo di Tura</a> recorded his experience from <a href="/wiki/Siena" title="Siena">Siena</a>, where plague arrived in May 1348:
</p>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r996844942" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>Father abandoned child, wife husband, one brother another; for this illness seemed to strike through the breath and sight. And so they died. And none could be found to bury the dead for money or friendship. Members of a household brought their dead to a ditch as best they could, without priest, without divine offices ... great pits were dug and piled deep with the multitude of dead. And they died by the hundreds both day and night ... And as soon as those ditches were filled more were dug ... And I, Agnolo di Tura ... buried my five children with my own hands. And there were also those who were so sparsely covered with earth that the dogs dragged them forth and devoured many bodies throughout the city. There was no one who wept for any death, for all awaited death. And so many died that all believed it was the end of the world.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-142"><a href="#cite_note-142">[133]</a></sup></p></blockquote>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Economic">Economic</span></h4>
<p>With such a large population decline from the pandemic, wages soared in response to a labour shortage.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEScheidel2017292–93,_304_143-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEScheidel2017292–93,_304-143">[134]</a></sup> On the other hand, in the quarter century after the Black Death in England, it is clear many labourers, artisans, and craftsmen, those living from money-wages alone, did suffer a reduction in real incomes owing to rampant inflation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMunro2004352_144-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMunro2004352-144">[135]</a></sup> Landowners were also pushed to substitute monetary rents for labour services in an effort to keep tenants.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-145"><a href="#cite_note-145">[136]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Environmental">Environmental</span></h4>
<p>A study performed by Thomas Van Hoof of the Utrecht University suggests that the innumerable deaths brought on by the pandemic cooled the climate by freeing up land and triggering <a href="/wiki/Reforestation" title="Reforestation">reforestation</a>. This may have led to the <a href="/wiki/Little_Ice_Age" title="Little Ice Age">Little Ice Age</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-146"><a href="#cite_note-146">[137]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Persecutions">Persecutions</span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jewish_persecutions_during_the_Black_Death" title="Jewish persecutions during the Black Death">Jewish persecutions during the Black Death</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Doutielt1.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="439" data-file-width="736" decoding="async" height="131" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a9/Doutielt1.jpg/220px-Doutielt1.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a9/Doutielt1.jpg/330px-Doutielt1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a9/Doutielt1.jpg/440px-Doutielt1.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Doutielt1.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Jews being <a href="/wiki/Death_by_burning" title="Death by burning">burned at the stake</a> in 1349. Miniature from a 14th-century manuscript <i>Antiquitates Flandriae</i></div></div></div>
<p>Renewed religious fervour and <a href="/wiki/Fanaticism" title="Fanaticism">fanaticism</a> bloomed in the wake of the Black Death. Some Europeans targeted "various groups such as Jews, <a href="/wiki/Friar" title="Friar">friars</a>, foreigners, beggars, <a href="/wiki/Pilgrim" title="Pilgrim">pilgrims</a>", lepers,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENirenberg1998_147-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENirenberg1998-147">[138]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMoore1987_148-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMoore1987-148">[139]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Romani_people" title="Romani people">Romani</a>, blaming them for the crisis. <a href="/wiki/Leprosy" title="Leprosy">Lepers</a>, and others with skin diseases such as <a href="/wiki/Acne" title="Acne">acne</a> or <a href="/wiki/Psoriasis" title="Psoriasis">psoriasis</a>, were killed throughout Europe.
</p><p>Because 14th-century healers and governments were at a loss to explain or stop the disease, Europeans turned to <a href="/wiki/Astrology" title="Astrology">astrological</a> forces, earthquakes, and the <a href="/wiki/Well_poisoning#Medieval_accusations_against_Jews" title="Well poisoning">poisoning of wells by Jews</a> as possible reasons for outbreaks.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBennettHollister2006326_19-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBennettHollister2006326-19">[17]</a></sup> Many believed the epidemic was a <a href="/wiki/Divine_retribution" title="Divine retribution">punishment by God</a> for their sins, and could be relieved by winning <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/God%27s_forgiveness" title="God's forgiveness">God's forgiveness</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-149"><a href="#cite_note-149">[140]</a></sup>
</p><p>There were many attacks against Jewish communities.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-JewishEncyclopedia_150-0"><a href="#cite_note-JewishEncyclopedia-150">[141]</a></sup> In the <a href="/wiki/Strasbourg_massacre" title="Strasbourg massacre">Strasbourg massacre</a> of February 1349, about 2,000 Jews were murdered.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-JewishEncyclopedia_150-1"><a href="#cite_note-JewishEncyclopedia-150">[141]</a></sup> In August 1349, the Jewish communities in <a href="/wiki/Mainz" title="Mainz">Mainz</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cologne" title="Cologne">Cologne</a> were annihilated. By 1351, 60 major and 150 smaller Jewish communities had been destroyed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-151"><a href="#cite_note-151">[142]</a></sup> During this period many Jews relocated to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Poland_(1025%E2%80%931385)" title="Kingdom of Poland (1025–1385)">Poland</a>, where they received a warm welcome from King <a href="/wiki/Casimir_III_the_Great" title="Casimir III the Great">Casimir the Great</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGottfried201074_152-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGottfried201074-152">[143]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Social">Social</span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:292px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:The_Triumph_of_Death_by_Pieter_Bruegel_the_Elder.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1517" data-file-width="2126" decoding="async" height="207" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b3/The_Triumph_of_Death_by_Pieter_Bruegel_the_Elder.jpg/290px-The_Triumph_of_Death_by_Pieter_Bruegel_the_Elder.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b3/The_Triumph_of_Death_by_Pieter_Bruegel_the_Elder.jpg/435px-The_Triumph_of_Death_by_Pieter_Bruegel_the_Elder.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b3/The_Triumph_of_Death_by_Pieter_Bruegel_the_Elder.jpg/580px-The_Triumph_of_Death_by_Pieter_Bruegel_the_Elder.jpg 2x" width="290"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:The_Triumph_of_Death_by_Pieter_Bruegel_the_Elder.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pieter_Brueghel_the_Elder" title="Pieter Brueghel the Elder">Pieter Bruegel</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/The_Triumph_of_Death" title="The Triumph of Death">The Triumph of Death</a></i> reflects the social upheaval and terror that followed plague, which devastated medieval Europe.</div></div></div>
<p>One theory that has been advanced is that the devastation in <a href="/wiki/Florence" title="Florence">Florence</a> caused by the Black Death, which hit Europe between 1348 and 1350, resulted in a shift in the world view of people in 14th-century Italy and led to the <a href="/wiki/Renaissance" title="Renaissance">Renaissance</a>. Italy was particularly badly hit by the pandemic, and it has been speculated that the resulting familiarity with death caused thinkers to dwell more on their lives on Earth, rather than on <a href="/wiki/Spirituality" title="Spirituality">spirituality</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Afterlife" title="Afterlife">afterlife</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETuchman1978_153-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETuchman1978-153">[144]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-155"><a href="#cite_note-155">[j]</a></sup> It has also been argued that the Black Death prompted a new wave of piety, manifested in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Patron#Arts" title="Patron">sponsorship</a> of religious works of art.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-156"><a href="#cite_note-156">[146]</a></sup>
</p><p>This does not fully explain why the Renaissance occurred in Italy in the 14th century. The Black Death was a pandemic that affected all of Europe in the ways described, not only Italy. The Renaissance's emergence in Italy was most likely the result of the complex interaction of the above factors,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrotton2006_157-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrotton2006-157">[147]</a></sup> in combination with an <a href="/wiki/Greek_scholars_in_the_Renaissance#Contribution_of_Greek_scholars_to_the_Italian_Renaissance" title="Greek scholars in the Renaissance">influx of Greek scholars</a> following the <a href="/wiki/Fall_of_Constantinople#Impact_on_the_Renaissance" title="Fall of Constantinople">fall of the Byzantine Empire</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2020)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> As a result of the drastic reduction in the populace the value of the working class increased, and commoners came to enjoy more freedom. To answer the increased need for labour, workers travelled in search of the most favorable position economically.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENetzley1998_158-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENetzley1998-158">[148]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template noprint noexcerpt Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wikipedia:NOTRS" title="Wikipedia:NOTRS"><span title="This claim needs references to better sources. (April 2020)">better source needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>Prior to the emergence of the Black Death, the workings of Europe were run by the Catholic Church and the continent was considered a feudalistic society, composed of <a href="/wiki/Fief" title="Fief">fiefs</a> and city-states.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_159-0"><a href="#cite_note-:0-159">[149]</a></sup> The pandemic completely restructured both religion and political forces; survivors began to turn to other forms of spirituality and the power dynamics of the fiefs and city-states crumbled.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_159-1"><a href="#cite_note-:0-159">[149]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-160"><a href="#cite_note-160">[150]</a></sup>
</p><p>Cairo's population, partly owing to the numerous plague epidemics, was in the early 18th century half of what it was in 1347.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:7_113-4"><a href="#cite_note-:7-113">[108]</a></sup> The populations of some Italian cities, notably <a href="/wiki/Florence" title="Florence">Florence</a>, did not regain their pre-14th century size until the 19th century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENauert2006106_161-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENauert2006106-161">[151]</a></sup> The demographic decline due to the pandemic had economic consequences: the prices of food dropped and land values declined by 30–40% in most parts of Europe between 1350 and 1400.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-162"><a href="#cite_note-162">[152]</a></sup> Landholders faced a great loss, but for ordinary men and women it was a windfall. The survivors of the pandemic found not only that the prices of food were lower but also that lands were more abundant, and many of them inherited property from their dead relatives, and this probably destabilized <a href="/wiki/Feudalism" title="Feudalism">feudalism</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-163"><a href="#cite_note-163">[153]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHaddockKiesling2002_164-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHaddockKiesling2002-164">[154]</a></sup>
</p><p>The word "<a href="/wiki/Quarantine" title="Quarantine">quarantine</a>" has its roots in this period, though the concept of isolating people to prevent the spread of disease is older. In the city-state of <a href="/wiki/Republic_of_Ragusa" title="Republic of Ragusa">Ragusa</a> (modern <a href="/wiki/Dubrovnik" title="Dubrovnik">Dubrovnik</a>, Croatia), a thirty-day isolation period was implemented in 1377 for new arrivals to the city from plague-affected areas. The isolation period was later extended to forty days, and given the name "quarantino" from the Italian word for "forty".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sehdev_165-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sehdev-165">[155]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Recurrences">Recurrences</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Second_plague_pandemic">Second plague pandemic</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Second_plague_pandemic" title="Second plague pandemic">Second plague pandemic</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Great_plague_of_london-1665.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="529" data-file-width="380" decoding="async" height="306" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1e/Great_plague_of_london-1665.jpg/220px-Great_plague_of_london-1665.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1e/Great_plague_of_london-1665.jpg/330px-Great_plague_of_london-1665.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/1/1e/Great_plague_of_london-1665.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Great_plague_of_london-1665.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Great_Plague_of_London" title="Great Plague of London">Great Plague of London</a>, in 1665, killed up to 100,000 people.</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Paul_F%C3%BCrst,_Der_Doctor_Schnabel_von_Rom_(coloured_version).png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="798" data-file-width="570" decoding="async" height="308" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ea/Paul_F%C3%BCrst%2C_Der_Doctor_Schnabel_von_Rom_%28coloured_version%29.png/220px-Paul_F%C3%BCrst%2C_Der_Doctor_Schnabel_von_Rom_%28coloured_version%29.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ea/Paul_F%C3%BCrst%2C_Der_Doctor_Schnabel_von_Rom_%28coloured_version%29.png/330px-Paul_F%C3%BCrst%2C_Der_Doctor_Schnabel_von_Rom_%28coloured_version%29.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ea/Paul_F%C3%BCrst%2C_Der_Doctor_Schnabel_von_Rom_%28coloured_version%29.png/440px-Paul_F%C3%BCrst%2C_Der_Doctor_Schnabel_von_Rom_%28coloured_version%29.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Paul_F%C3%BCrst,_Der_Doctor_Schnabel_von_Rom_(coloured_version).png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A <a href="/wiki/Plague_doctor" title="Plague doctor">plague doctor</a> and his typical <a href="/wiki/Plague_doctor_costume" title="Plague doctor costume">apparel</a> during the 17th Century Outbreak.</div></div></div>
<p>The plague repeatedly returned to haunt Europe and the Mediterranean throughout the 14th to 17th centuries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPorter200925_166-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPorter200925-166">[156]</a></sup> According to Jean-Noël Biraben, the plague was present somewhere in Europe in every year between 1346 and 1671.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHays199858_167-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHays199858-167">[157]</a></sup> (Note that some researchers have cautions about the uncritical use of Biraben's data.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERoosenCurtis2018_168-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERoosenCurtis2018-168">[158]</a></sup>) The <a href="/wiki/Second_plague_pandemic" title="Second plague pandemic">second pandemic</a> was particularly widespread in the following years: 1360–63; 1374; 1400; 1438–39; 1456–57; 1464–66; 1481–85; 1500–03; 1518–31; 1544–48; 1563–66; 1573–88; 1596–99; 1602–11; 1623–40; 1644–54; and 1664–67. Subsequent outbreaks, though severe, marked the retreat from most of Europe (18th century) and northern Africa (19th century).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHays200546_169-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHays200546-169">[159]</a></sup> The historian George Sussman argued that the plague had not occurred in East Africa until the 1900s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESussman2011_84-4"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESussman2011-84">[80]</a></sup> However, other sources suggest that the Second pandemic did indeed reach Sub-Saharan Africa.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGreen2018_111-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGreen2018-111">[106]</a></sup>
</p><p>According to historian <a href="/wiki/Geoffrey_Parker_(historian)" title="Geoffrey Parker (historian)">Geoffrey Parker</a>, "France alone lost almost a million people to the plague in the epidemic of 1628–31."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEParker20017_170-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEParker20017-170">[160]</a></sup> In the first half of the 17th century, a plague killed some 1.7 million people in Italy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-171"><a href="#cite_note-171">[161]</a></sup> More than 1.25 million deaths resulted from the extreme incidence of plague in 17th-century <a href="/wiki/Habsburg_Spain" title="Habsburg Spain">Spain</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPayne1973Chapter_15:_The_Seventeenth-Century_Decline_172-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPayne1973Chapter_15:_The_Seventeenth-Century_Decline-172">[162]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Chevalier_Roze_%C3%A0_la_Tourette_-_1720.PNG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1087" data-file-width="1668" decoding="async" height="143" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5b/Chevalier_Roze_%C3%A0_la_Tourette_-_1720.PNG/220px-Chevalier_Roze_%C3%A0_la_Tourette_-_1720.PNG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5b/Chevalier_Roze_%C3%A0_la_Tourette_-_1720.PNG/330px-Chevalier_Roze_%C3%A0_la_Tourette_-_1720.PNG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5b/Chevalier_Roze_%C3%A0_la_Tourette_-_1720.PNG/440px-Chevalier_Roze_%C3%A0_la_Tourette_-_1720.PNG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Chevalier_Roze_%C3%A0_la_Tourette_-_1720.PNG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Contemporaneous painting of <a href="/wiki/Marseille" title="Marseille">Marseille</a> during the <a href="/wiki/Great_Plague_of_Marseille" title="Great Plague of Marseille">Great Plague</a> in 1720</div></div></div>
<p>The Black Death ravaged much of the <a href="/wiki/Muslim_world" title="Muslim world">Islamic world</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-173"><a href="#cite_note-173">[163]</a></sup> Plague was present in at least one location in the Islamic world virtually every year between 1500 and 1850.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-174"><a href="#cite_note-174">[164]</a></sup> Plague repeatedly struck the cities of North Africa. <a href="/wiki/Algiers" title="Algiers">Algiers</a> lost 30,000–50,000 inhabitants to it in 1620–21, and again in 1654–57, 1665, 1691, and 1740–42.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDavis2004_175-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDavis2004-175">[165]</a></sup> Cairo suffered more than fifty plague epidemics within 150 years from the plague's first appearance, with the final outbreak of the second pandemic there in the 1840s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:7_113-5"><a href="#cite_note-:7-113">[108]</a></sup> Plague remained a major event in <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_Empire" title="Ottoman Empire">Ottoman</a> society until the second quarter of the 19th century. Between 1701 and 1750, thirty-seven larger and smaller epidemics were recorded in <a href="/wiki/Constantinople" title="Constantinople">Constantinople</a>, and an additional thirty-one between 1751 and 1800.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-176"><a href="#cite_note-176">[166]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Baghdad" title="Baghdad">Baghdad</a> has suffered severely from visitations of the plague, and sometimes two-thirds of its population had died.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEIssawi198899_177-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEIssawi198899-177">[167]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Third_plague_pandemic">Third plague pandemic</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Third_plague_pandemic" title="Third plague pandemic">Third plague pandemic</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:World_distribution_of_plague_1998.PNG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="469" data-file-width="700" decoding="async" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6b/World_distribution_of_plague_1998.PNG/220px-World_distribution_of_plague_1998.PNG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6b/World_distribution_of_plague_1998.PNG/330px-World_distribution_of_plague_1998.PNG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6b/World_distribution_of_plague_1998.PNG/440px-World_distribution_of_plague_1998.PNG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:World_distribution_of_plague_1998.PNG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Worldwide distribution of plague-infected animals, 1998</div></div></div>
<p>The third plague pandemic (1855–1859) started in China in the mid-19th century, spreading to all inhabited continents and killing 10 million people in India alone.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-178"><a href="#cite_note-178">[168]</a></sup> The investigation of the pathogen that caused the 19th-century plague was begun by teams of scientists who visited Hong Kong in 1894, among whom was the French-Swiss bacteriologist <a href="/wiki/Alexandre_Yersin" title="Alexandre Yersin">Alexandre Yersin</a>, after whom the pathogen was named.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChristakosOleaSerreWang2005110–14_29-5"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChristakosOleaSerreWang2005110–14-29">[27]</a></sup>
</p><p>Twelve plague outbreaks in Australia between 1900 and 1925 resulted in well over 1,000 deaths, chiefly in Sydney. This led to the establishment of a Public Health Department there which undertook some leading-edge research on plague transmission from rat fleas to humans via the bacillus <i>Yersinia pestis</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-179"><a href="#cite_note-179">[169]</a></sup>
</p><p>The first North American plague epidemic was the <a href="/wiki/San_Francisco_plague_of_1900%E2%80%931904" title="San Francisco plague of 1900–1904">San Francisco plague of 1900–1904</a>, followed by another outbreak in 1907–1908.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChase2004_180-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChase2004-180">[170]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEchenberg2007_181-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEchenberg2007-181">[171]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKraut1995_182-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKraut1995-182">[172]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Modern-day">Modern-day</span></h3>
<p>Modern treatment methods include <a href="/wiki/Insecticide" title="Insecticide">insecticides</a>, the use of <a href="/wiki/Antibiotic" title="Antibiotic">antibiotics</a>, and a <a href="/wiki/Waldemar_Haffkine#Anti-plague_vaccine" title="Waldemar Haffkine">plague vaccine</a>. It is feared that the plague bacterium could develop <a href="/wiki/Drug_resistance" title="Drug resistance">drug resistance</a> and again become a major health threat. One case of a drug-resistant form of the bacterium was found in <a href="/wiki/Madagascar" title="Madagascar">Madagascar</a> in 1995.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-183"><a href="#cite_note-183">[173]</a></sup> A further outbreak in Madagascar was reported in November 2014.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-184"><a href="#cite_note-184">[174]</a></sup> In October 2017, the <a href="/wiki/21st_century_Madagascar_plague_outbreaks" title="21st century Madagascar plague outbreaks">deadliest outbreak of the plague</a> in modern times hit Madagascar, killing 170 people and infecting thousands.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-185"><a href="#cite_note-185">[175]</a></sup>
</p><p>An estimate of the <a href="/wiki/Case_fatality_rate" title="Case fatality rate">case fatality rate</a> for the modern <a href="/wiki/Bubonic_plague" title="Bubonic plague">bubonic plague</a>, following the introduction of <a href="/wiki/Antibiotic" title="Antibiotic">antibiotics</a>, is 11%, although it may be higher in underdeveloped regions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-186"><a href="#cite_note-186">[176]</a></sup>
</p>

<li id="cite_note-2"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-2">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Other names include <i>Great Mortality</i> (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Latin_language" title="Latin language">Latin</a>: <i lang="la">magna mortalitas</i>, <small><a href="/wiki/Literal_translation" title="Literal translation">lit.</a> </small>'Great Death', common in the 14th century), <span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">atra mors</i></span>, 'black death', the Great Plague, the Great Bubonic Plague or the Black Plague.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-16"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-16">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Declining temperatures following the end of the <a href="/wiki/Medieval_Warm_Period" title="Medieval Warm Period">Medieval Warm Period</a> added to the crisis</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-30"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-30">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">He was able to adopt the epidemiology of the bubonic plague for the Black Death for the second edition in 1908, implicating rats and fleas in the process, and his interpretation was widely accepted for other ancient and medieval epidemics, such as the <a href="/wiki/Plague_of_Justinian" title="Plague of Justinian">Plague of Justinian</a> that was prevalent in the <a href="/wiki/Byzantine_Empire" title="Byzantine Empire">Eastern Roman Empire</a> from 541 to 700 CE.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChristakosOleaSerreWang2005110–14_29-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChristakosOleaSerreWang2005110–14-29">[27]</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-54"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-54">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">In 1998, Drancourt et al. reported the detection of <i>Y. pestis</i> DNA in human dental pulp from a medieval grave.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[49]</a></sup> Another team led by <a href="/wiki/Marcus_Thomas_Pius_Gilbert" title="Marcus Thomas Pius Gilbert">Tom Gilbert</a> cast doubt on this identification<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGilbertCuccuiWhiteLynnerup2004_53-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGilbertCuccuiWhiteLynnerup2004-53">[50]</a></sup> and the techniques employed, stating that this method "does not allow us to confirm the identification of Y. pestis as the <a href="/wiki/Etiology" title="Etiology">aetiological</a> agent of the Black Death and subsequent plagues. In addition, the utility of the published tooth-based ancient DNA technique used to diagnose fatal <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bacteremia" title="Bacteremia">bacteraemias</a> in historical epidemics still awaits independent corroboration".</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-109"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-109">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">However, other researchers do not think that plague ever became endemic in Europe or its rat population. The disease repeatedly wiped out the rodent carriers, so that the fleas died out until a new outbreak from Central Asia repeated the process. The outbreaks have been shown to occur roughly 15 years after a warmer and wetter period in areas where plague is endemic in other species, such as <a href="/wiki/Gerbillinae" title="Gerbillinae">gerbils</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-107"><a href="#cite_note-107">[103]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESchmidBüntgenEasterdayGinzler2015_108-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESchmidBüntgenEasterdayGinzler2015-108">[104]</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-120"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-120">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The only medical detail that is questionable in Boccaccio's description is that the gavocciolo was an "infallible token of approaching death", as, if the bubo discharges, recovery is possible.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEZiegler199818–19_119-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEZiegler199818–19-119">[114]</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-131"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-131">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">According to medieval historian <a href="/wiki/Philip_Daileader" title="Philip Daileader">Philip Daileader</a>,<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r996844942" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><blockquote class="templatequote" style="font-size:100%"><p> The trend of recent research is pointing to a figure more like 45–50% of the European population dying during a four-year period. There is a fair amount of geographic variation. In Mediterranean Europe, areas such as Italy, the south of France and Spain, where plague ran for about four years consecutively, it was probably closer to 75–80% of the population. In Germany and England ... it was probably closer to 20%.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Daileader_130-1"><a href="#cite_note-Daileader-130">[124]</a></sup></p></blockquote></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-133"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-133">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Norwegian historian Ole Benedictow suggests: <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r996844942" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><blockquote class="templatequote" style="font-size:100%"><p> Detailed study of the mortality data available points to two conspicuous features in relation to the mortality caused by the Black Death: namely the extreme level of mortality caused by the Black Death, and the remarkable similarity or consistency of the level of mortality, from Spain in southern Europe to England in north-western Europe. The data is sufficiently widespread and numerous to make it likely that the Black Death swept away around 60% of Europe's population. The generally assumed population of Europe at the time is about 80 million, implying that around 50 million people died in the Black Death.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Benedictow_132-1"><a href="#cite_note-Benedictow-132">[125]</a></sup></p></blockquote></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-136"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-136">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">While contemporary accounts report mass burial pits being created in response to the large number of dead, recent scientific investigations of a burial pit in Central London found well-preserved individuals to be buried in isolated, evenly spaced graves, suggesting at least some pre-planning and Christian burials at this time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDickPringleSloaneCarver2015_135-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDickPringleSloaneCarver2015-135">[127]</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-155"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-155">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The Black Death caused greater upheaval to Florence's social and political structure than later epidemics. Despite a significant number of deaths among members of the ruling classes, the government of Florence continued to function during this period. Formal meetings of elected representatives were suspended during the height of the epidemic due to the chaotic conditions in the city, but a small group of officials was appointed to conduct the affairs of the city, which ensured continuity of government.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHattyHatty199989_154-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHattyHatty199989-154">[145]</a></sup></span>
</li>
</ol></div></div>

<li id="cite_note-21"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-21">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation book cs1" id="CITEREFPontoppidan1755">Pontoppidan E (1755). <a class="external text" href="https://archive.org/details/naturalhistoryNc2Pont/page/n57" rel="nofollow"><i>The Natural History of Norway: …</i></a>. London: A. Linde. p. 24.</cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=The+Natural+History+of+Norway%3A+%E2%80%A6&amp;rft.place=London&amp;rft.pages=24&amp;rft.pub=A.+Linde&amp;rft.date=1755&amp;rft.aulast=Pontoppidan&amp;rft.aufirst=E&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Farchive.org%2Fdetails%2FnaturalhistoryNc2Pont%2Fpage%2Fn57&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ABlack+Death"></span> From p. 24: "Norway, indeed, cannot be said to be entirely exempt from pestilential distempers, for the Black-death, known all over Europe by its terrible ravages, from the years 1348 to 50, was felt here as in other parts, and to the great diminution of the number of the inhabitants."</span>

<li id="cite_note-:2-22"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-:2_22-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-:2_22-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-:2_22-2"><sup><i><b>c</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-:2_22-3"><sup><i><b>d</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation journal cs1" id="CITEREFd'Irsay1926">d'Irsay S (1926). <li id="cite_note-26"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-26">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation book cs1 cs1-prop-foreign-lang-source" id="CITEREFde_Corbeil1907">de Corbeil G (1907) [1200].  Valentin R (ed.). <a class="external text" href="https://archive.org/details/egidiicorbolien02rosegoog" rel="nofollow"><i>Egidii Corboliensis Viaticus: De signis et symptomatibus aegritudium</i></a>. <i>Bibliotheca scriptorum medii aevi Teubneriana</i> (in Latin). Harvard University: In aedibus B.G. Teubneri.</cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=Egidii+Corboliensis+Viaticus%3A+De+signis+et+symptomatibus+aegritudium&amp;rft.place=Harvard+University&amp;rft.pub=In+aedibus+B.G.+Teubneri&amp;rft.date=1907&amp;rft.aulast=de+Corbeil&amp;rft.aufirst=G&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Farchive.org%2Fdetails%2Fegidiicorbolien02rosegoog&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ABlack+Death"></span></span>

<li id="cite_note-27"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-27">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">On page 22 of the manuscript in <a class="external text" href="http://gallica.bnf.fr/ark:/12148/btv1b9078277z/f25.image" rel="nofollow">Gallica</a> <a class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20161006064435/http://gallica.bnf.fr/ark:/12148/btv1b9078277z/f25.image" rel="nofollow">Archived</a> 6 October 2016 at the <a href="/wiki/Wayback_Machine" title="Wayback Machine">Wayback Machine</a>, Simon mentions the phrase "<i>mors nigra</i>" (Black Death): "<i>Cum rex finisset oracula judiciorum / Mors nigra surrexit, et gentes reddidit illi</i>;" (When the king ended the oracles of judgment / Black Death arose, and the nations surrendered to him;).
<ul><li>A more legible copy of the poem appears in: Emile Littré (1841) <a class="external text" href="http://www.persee.fr/web/revues/home/prescript/article/bec_0373-6237_1841_num_2_1_451584?_Prescripts_Search_tabs1=standard&amp;" rel="nofollow">"Opuscule relatif à la peste de 1348, composé par un contemporain"</a> <a class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20140722010105/http://www.persee.fr/web/revues/home/prescript/article/bec_0373-6237_1841_num_2_1_451584?_Prescripts_Search_tabs1=standard&amp;" rel="nofollow">Archived</a> 22 July 2014 at the <a href="/wiki/Wayback_Machine" title="Wayback Machine">Wayback Machine</a> (Work concerning the plague of 1348, composed by a contemporary), <i>Bibliothèque de l'école des chartes</i>, <b>2</b> (2) : 201–43; see especially p. 228.</li>
<li>See also: Joseph Patrick Byrne, <i>The Black Death</i> (Westport, Connecticut: Greenwood Press, 2004), <a class="external text" href="https://books.google.com/books?id=yw3HmjRvVQMC&amp;pg=PA1" rel="nofollow">p. 1.</a> <a class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20160426053818/https://books.google.com/books?id=yw3HmjRvVQMC&amp;pg=PA1" rel="nofollow">Archived</a> 26 April 2016 at the <a href="/wiki/Wayback_Machine" title="Wayback Machine">Wayback Machine</a></li></ul>
</span></li>

<li id="cite_note-77"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-77">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation news cs1" id="CITEREFWade2010"><a href="/wiki/Nicholas_Wade" title="Nicholas Wade">Wade N</a> (31 October 2010). <a class="external text" href="https://www.nytimes.com/2010/11/01/health/01plague.html" rel="nofollow">"Europe's Plagues Came From China, Study Finds"</a>. <i><a href="/wiki/The_New_York_Times" title="The New York Times">The New York Times</a></i>. <a class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20101104083917/http://www.nytimes.com/2010/11/01/health/01plague.html" rel="nofollow">Archived</a> from the original on 4 November 2010<span class="reference-accessdate">. Retrieved <span class="nowrap">25 March</span> 2020</span>. <q>The great waves of plague that twice devastated Europe and changed the course of history had their origins in China, a team of medical geneticists reported Sunday, as did a third plague outbreak that struck less harmfully in the 19th century. ... In the issue of <i><a href="/wiki/Nature_Genetics" title="Nature Genetics">Nature Genetics</a></i> published online Sunday, they conclude that all three of the great waves of plague originated from China, where the root of their tree is situated. ... The likely origin of the plague in China has nothing to do with its people or crowded cities, Dr. Achtman said. The bacterium has no interest in people, whom it slaughters by accident. Its natural hosts are various species of rodent such as marmots and voles, which are found throughout China.</q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft.jtitle=The+New+York+Times&amp;rft.atitle=Europe%27s+Plagues+Came+From+China%2C+Study+Finds&amp;rft.date=2010-10-31&amp;rft.aulast=Wade&amp;rft.aufirst=N&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fwww.nytimes.com%2F2010%2F11%2F01%2Fhealth%2F01plague.html&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ABlack+Death"></span></span>

<li id="cite_note-92"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-92">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation book cs1 cs1-prop-foreign-lang-source" id="CITEREFBaker1847">Baker G (1847) [1350].  Gilles AJ (ed.). <a class="external text" href="https://archive.org/details/galfridilebaker00gilegoog/page/n27" rel="nofollow"><i>Galfridi Le Baker de Swinbroke, Chronicon Angliae temporibus Edwardi II et Edwardi III</i></a> (in Latin and English). Londini: apud Jacobum Bohn. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/LCCN_(identifier)" title="LCCN (identifier)">LCCN</a> <a class="external text" href="//lccn.loc.gov/08014593" rel="nofollow">08014593</a>. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/OL_(identifier)" title="OL (identifier)">OL</a> <a class="external text" href="//openlibrary.org/books/OL6996785M" rel="nofollow">6996785M</a>. Archived from <a class="external text" href="https://archive.org/details/galfridilebaker00gilegoog" rel="nofollow">the original</a> on 3 August 2008 – via <a href="/wiki/Internet_Archive" title="Internet Archive">Internet Archive</a>.</cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=Galfridi+Le+Baker+de+Swinbroke%2C+Chronicon+Angliae+temporibus+Edwardi+II+et+Edwardi+III&amp;rft.place=Londini&amp;rft.pub=apud+Jacobum+Bohn&amp;rft.date=1847&amp;rft_id=%2F%2Fopenlibrary.org%2Fbooks%2FOL6996785M%23id-name%3DOL&amp;rft_id=info%3Alccn%2F08014593&amp;rft.aulast=Baker&amp;rft.aufirst=G&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Farchive.org%2Fdetails%2Fgalfridilebaker00gilegoog&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ABlack+Death"></span></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-FOOTNOTEWheelis2002-93"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWheelis2002_93-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="#CITEREFWheelis2002">Wheelis 2002</a>.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-FOOTNOTEBarrasGreub2014-94"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBarrasGreub2014_94-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="#CITEREFBarrasGreub2014">Barras &amp; Greub 2014</a>"In the Middle Ages, a famous although controversial example is offered by the siege of Caffa (now Feodossia in Ukraine/Crimea), a Genovese outpost on the Black Sea coast, by the Mongols. In 1346, the attacking army experienced an epidemic of bubonic plague. The Italian chronicler Gabriele de' Mussi, in his <i>Istoria de Morbo sive Mortalitate quae fuit Anno Domini 1348</i>, describes quite plausibly how plague was transmitted by the Mongols by throwing diseased cadavers with catapults into the besieged city, and how ships transporting Genovese soldiers, fleas and rats fleeing from there brought it to the Mediterranean ports. Given the highly complex epidemiology of plague, this interpretation of the Black Death (which might have killed &gt;25 million people in the following years throughout Europe) as stemming from a specific and localized origin of the Black Death remains controversial. Similarly, it remains doubtful whether the effect of throwing infected cadavers could have been the sole cause of the outburst of an epidemic in the besieged city."</span>
</li>
